A LARGE BONUS.

Policy being for 10,000, and issued in 1886, on the five year distribution plan. This

This pamphlet is specially written to show a method of
counteracting the ill effects of indiscretions in early life,
over-work, mental worry, &c., and contains a fund of use-
ful information for every sufferer from the following com-
plaints, viz Loss of Energy and Will Power, NERVOUS
DEBILITY, Pains in the Back, Defective Memory, Specks
before the Eyes, Excessive Nervousness, Premature
Decay, Bashfulness, Excessive Heart Palpitation, Des-
pondency, Impure Blood, Liver and Kidney Complaints,
Contagious Diseases, and various other symptoms experi-
enced by Nervous Sufferers.
This Pamphlet is sent Free on Application,
and all in any way suffering from the above would derive
benefit from the perusal of this work.

become perfectly acquainted with the most approved scientific and successful methods of affecting their cure.

==J
This eminent Specialist, Founder, and Chief
Surgeon of the long-established and favourably
known Dispensary that now bears his name, re-
ceived his medical education in the leading colleges
and hospitals of Europe. He has devoted a life-
time to the study of
PRIVATE, NERVOUS. AND SKIN
DISEASES.
He has by profound research thoroughly investi-
gated their origin, traced their deadly sources and
insidious dissemination, and ascertained the surest
and safest methods by which they may be eradi-
cated, with these widely recognised qualifications
combined with unswerving faithfulness to hi *
patients, he has during his practice, extending
over FORTY YEARS, attained a well-deserved
pre-eminence in their treatment. His practice
embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.
YOUNG MEN
who suffer from the results of early follies, trans-
gressions or excesses of more nature age, bladder
difficulties, a whitish or dark ropy sediment in
the water, accompanied by slight burning or
smarting sensation, Headaches, Drowsiness during
the day, circles round the eyes, Giddiness,
irregularity of the bowels, specks before the eyes,
bashfulness, discontentedness, weakness or pains
in the back. Liver or kidney complaints, dreams,
buzzing noises in the cars and head, pimples, a
fear that something dreadful is about to happen,
weak stomach, yellow or blood-shot eyes, impure
blood, hasty, uncontrollable temper, hacking
cough, who cannot marry, nervousness in society,
wakefulness, palpitation of the heart, general
weakness, and other symptoms which lead to in-
sanity and death, should consult him, for he has
mastered these difficulties. On receipt of 6d. in
stamps he will send secure from observation in a
plain wrapper, postage
P FREE, A LARGE CLOTH-BOUND
BOOK.
Showing plainly how all diseases of the private br
nervous system may be cured thoroughly and per-
manently without unnecessary expence. This
book will prove a valuable companion, and even
though you be perfectly well its general inform-
tion and prescription may assist you in restoring
some poor friend to health even after his case has
been pronounced incurable by his medical at-
tendant.
ONE POUND IS SUFFICIENT TO
SEND
for Remedies necessary for the cure of NER-
VOUS DEBILITY. Should you require medical
treatment, as procrastination is sometimes dan*.
gerous„it would be advisable for you to sit down
quietly*in your chamber and write to the doctor,
detailing every experienced symptom. He will
on receipt of such a letter, write you, promptly
and courteously, a candid and straightforward
oponion of your case. If you do not think you
understand the case, send for his famous medical
work the price of which is but sixpence ; enclosed
in the volume will be found a list of carefully-pre-
pared questions, which will, in all probability,
assist you materially in describing your ailment.
Dr Richards uses nothing but the purest remedies,
and never prescribes mercury under any circum-
stances The moment letters are carefully per-
used and answered they are destroyed by fire,
and patients may rest assured that their cases
will remain a secret between the doctor and
themselves. Dr. Richards has successfully treated

SATURDAY, JANUARY 30, 1892.
Government by Chaos.
The long-growing conviction that Party
Government is a failure has at last found
expression in an Australian Legislature.
In New South Wales, in recent times,
through the division of the House into three
sections instead of two as heretofore, Party
Government has been a worse failure than
usual, and the necessity for a change has
become more and more obvious. Therefore
an obscure member named G. D. Clark
drafted the following resolution, which was
duly relegated to an obscure corner of the
notice-paper, and looked upon by the old,
stereotyped politicians of the Assembly as
an unimportant fad which lay—in the old,
hackneyed phrase—beyond “the domain of
practical politics. ” And as practical politics
are generally held to consist only in un-
ivholesome scrambles after billets and office,
varied by efforts to secure some local conces-
sion for the township of Dry Forks or the
outlying settlement of Smith’s Curse, it may
be that these ancient men of routine and
dull monotony were perfectly right according
to their lights :
(1.) That the existing system of Party Government,
while enormously expensive to the country, manifestly
fails to secure the best possible results in the shape of
urgently required progressive legislation.
(2.) That it would facilitate the passing into law of
democratic measures if administrative and executive
functions were performed by a Committee elected by
Parliament.
(3.) That all questions of great national importance
should, before their final adoption, be submitted to a
vote of the entire adult population of the country.
* # *
Regarded as the first legislative sign of the
beginning of a new era in the art of govern-
ment this motion is a matter of some im-
portance. Apart from that, however, it has
no importance worth mentioning. Mr. 6.
D. Clark will probably be dead or will have
vanished out of politics before the reform is
fairly begun, and the same old motion will
come up for discussion 20, or perhaps 50,
times ere it produces any results. If Mr.
Clark succeeds in retaining his precarious
seat for 15 or 20 years, and, abandoning
cold-water hysterics, devotes himself solely
to this one subject—if ke becomes one of the
fearsome bores of the House, and raises his
topic on every possible occasion, and is
counted out almost every time he raises it—
if he hangs on to it till other members fly
from him in the refreshment-room, and
dodge him in the lobby, lest he should
button-hole them on the dreary question of
constitutional reform—and if he still sticks
to it till he becomes a veteran crank and an
object of derision to [the daily press—then
he may at last, in the course of years, see it
arrive within the domain of “ practical
politics,” and have the pleasure of knowing
that it has advanced a stage on the road to
success. After that some more prominent
politician will steal the idea and shunt Mr.
G. D. Clark, and refuse to recognise him
when they meet. The new politician will
stump the country on it, and will form a
party on the strength of it in the House,
and the daily press will gravely speculate as
to whether “ the time is yet ripe ” for such
a change, and then at last a Bill embodying
Mr. Clark’s idea will be passed, and the
Upper House—if there is one in these
days—will carefully throw it out. There-
upon there will be several more “ electoral
campaigns ” till the Upper House is coerced
into passing the measure, after which the
Governor—if there is still a Governor—will
probably “withhold his consent.” And, last
of all, the reform will get through in triumph,
and if Mr. Clark tries to assert in that far-
distant future that he is the father—in a
Parliamentary sense—of the great measure,
he will be thrown out as a base impostor,
while the country raises a monument to the
man who borrowed the idea just when it
promised to become successful. We men-
tion these things merely to encourage Mr.
G. D. Clark in his new path as a constitu-
tional reformer. The country is growing
tired of Party Government, but even when
the nation is absolutely howling for a great
reform, its progress is very slow ; when the
nation is not howling for it, its progress is
still slower.
* * *
The present system of Government is
a combination of so many impossibilities,
contradictions, and hopeless anachronisms
that its endurance for so many years is one
of the great political phenomena of the
age. In the first place, it pre-supposes
that every legislative body will be naturally
and permanently divided into two parties—
a supposition which is, in itself, a manifest
absurdity—yet the scheme can work success-
fully on no other basis. The people of New
South Wales are divided into something
like 150 separate religious sects, and
political sects are, if anything, still more
numerous. There are, in the present
Assembly, Freetrade Federationists and
Freetrade anti-Federationists, Protectionist
Federationists and Protectionist anti-Feder-
ationists, Freetrade Federationists who sup-
port Local Option, Freetrade Federationists
who do not support Local Option, Freetrade
anti-Federationists who are split in the same
fashion, Protectionist Federationists and
Protectionist anti-Federationists both simi-
larly sub-divided, Land Nationalisers who are
divided into Freetraders and Protectionists,
and sub-divided on Federation, Local Option,
and other questions, Socialists of all varieties,
and Labour members who are divided on the
fiscal issue, and sub-divided on the question
of Australian unity, and sub-sub-divided on
all the other questions already mentioned.
Also, there are Republicans who are split up
on the tariff question, and split some more
on the Labour question, and re-split on the
Federation question, and split over again on
the drink question, and all these varieties of
split-up Republicans are split still more on
every other question on record ; and there
are Imperialists who are divided into as
many varieties as the rest ; and, in addition
to all these, there are sectarian difficulties
arising out of the 150 different religions of
the province, and personal difficulties which
are about as many as all the previous
difficulties put together, and other difficulties
too numerous to mention. The final result
is that there are not, and never were, and
never will be even two men in the Assembly
who think sufficiently alike to form an
honest and conscientious party with a
workable programme. Yet out of all these
heterogeneous elements it is necessary to find
nine men who either think alike, or who will
falsely say that they do for the sake of the
salary, in order to form a Government; and
behind them it is necessary to find at least
62 more who will tell the same glaring
untruth for various other motives. And in
an equally heterogeneous Upper House it is
essential to raise a similar majority under
exactly the same difficulties, while in each
House the section that doesn’t profess to
believe everything which the Ministry says
that it believes is bound by the unwritten
laws of the constitution to profess to believe
everything which the leader of the Opposi-
tion says that he believes. And if the
House doesn’t divide itself into exactly two
sections, the result is chaos.
One of the most noteworthy features of
the system is that the people have practically
no voice in the election of the Ministry.
When a politician attains a certain distinc-
tion—no matter how—he becomes, in fact,
a practically irremoveable incubus. When
the alleged Freetrade party was uppermost
in New South Wales the Governor of the
period was wont to “send for” Parkes, and
however much the party and the portion of
the nation which it represented might object
to Parkes, it had to endure him or else let
the power pass into the hands of its
opponents. And when it had accepted
Parkes, as an unavoidable evil, for the
sake of Freetrade, it had to accept any
other individuals whom he chose to
appoint for the sake of Parkes. There
might not be in the whole Ministry one
single individual whom the party would have
chosen under a system of direct taxation,
but if it voted against them it thereby voted
against its principles, and therefore it en-
dured them as the only way in which it
could express its continued adhesion to a
Freetrade policy. And, having endured
them, it had also to endure all their works
however objectionable. It might loathe
every separate individual in the Cabinet ;
it might loathe their land policy, and their
public works policy, and their education
policy, and everything except their fiscal
policy, but so long as the party was a Free-
trade party it had to support the Freetrade
Government in all its iniquities, or else
overturn the Freetrade system. Therefore,
almost every Government goes in for land-
swindles and nepotism, and builds railways
through its own property, and sells its worth-
less allotments to the §tate for “reserves,”
and fakes contracts for its friends, because
it knows that the House cannot express its
disapproval of one of these things without
oversetting the entire Cabinet, and it hesi-
tates to do this because it can only get
another equally irresponsible Cabinet in
exchange.
# * *
The system of Party Government has no
single advantage to counterbalance all these
disabilities. The governing force of the
country, under the present policy, is solely
and entirely the dominant faction in the
Legislative Assembly. The country hires
this faction to do the work of legislation,
and hires the other faction to prevent it
doing it. It also hires the Legislative
Council to prevent it doing it, and it pays
the Governor for the same purpose, for the
only active exertion which is permitted him
is that of occasionally withholding his assent
to Acts of Parliament, and all his other
functions could be performed equally well
by a stuffed monkey. And, in addition
to all these, it keeps the Queen in the back-
ground as another agency to prevent it doing
it, for that gracious potentate’s authority
over Australia also consists solely in with-
holding her assent; and amid all these
obstacles there is much heavy toil in the
art and mystery of legislation, and the
national Sisyphus spends most of its time
in rolling ponderous bills uphill, and seeing
them roll down agfqn every time they ap-
proach the summit.
# # *
The scheme advanced years ago by The
Bulletin, and now submitted to the New
South Wales Parliament by Mr. Clark,
offers a remedy for at least a great part of
these numerous anomalies. It provides that
all Ministers shall be elected directly by a
vote of the Assembly, so that it will be
practicable to collect all the ablest represen-
tatives and combine them in one Cabinet,
quite irrespective of their political opinions.
Each man will administer his own depart-
ment, will draft what Bills he thinks fit, and
introduce them on his own responsibility,
and will be at liberty to criticise his col-
leagues or anybody else with absolute
freedom. The House will be at liberty to
reject or amend any or every proposition
brought forward by a Minister without
thereby expressing a “want of confidence” in
him ; it merely appoints him for a certain
period to do certain administrative work at
a fixed salary, but it does not pledge itself
by any means to endorse all his actions and
those of his colleagues under a penalty of
losing his services. Under this system it
could appoint Mr. M‘Millan to be Trea-
surer, if it had faith in his arithmetic, and
yet enforce a Protectionist policy, and the
fact of the House adopting such a policy and
directing the Treasurer to carry it out would
in no sense reflect upon his sincerity as a
Freetrader. His position would be just the
same as that of the Freetrade policeman
who continues to run in drunks with his old
vigour under a Protectionist regime, or the
Freetrade Governor who continued to
be a Governor under the same condi-
tions ; his private sentiments would be
his own affair, in which the Legis-
lature took no manner, of interest. The
system is one under which the nation would
make its own laws and appoint the Govern-
ment to carry them out, instead of the
Government appointing itself and then
making laws on its own account ; and it
would practically abolish the device of party,
because no Minister could do anything for
his party nor could any mere party do much
for him, and the old bond of self-interest
would therefore soon dissolve. Also, it would
wipe out no-confidence motions, for the
House would not be expected to have con-
fidence in the Cabinet at any time ; it would
extinguish the practice of the Ministry
“going to the country”; it would abolish
Government policies, for the nation itself
would supply the policy ; and it would give
every question a fair chance to be decided
on its own merits irrespective of party
interests. For all of which reasons it is one
of the most desirable reforms of the age, and
if only by reason of its desirability it will
probably come long after Mr. G. D. Clark
lias died of extreme old age and natural
decay.
Theatrical Management and the Press.
A question of considerable interest to the
Press at large and theatrical management in
general is involved in the present state of
affairs between the Melbourne Age and the
local Theatre Royal. Three weeks ago The
Bulletin published a paragraph accounting for
the careful omission from Age news columns of
any reference to the Melbourne Royal panto-
mime, subsequent to the appearance of a cold,
fault-finding, first-night notice. Mr. George
Coppin had cut down the list of newspaper
deadheads to the minimum liumbtslf of one from
each office, and the Age, being a high-minded,
liberal, honest, enterprising, unprejudiced
representative of “respectable” journalism—
in fact what is called a “ great” newspaper by
others of the same length, breadth and he ■
ness—had, in the expressive language of o 1
unorthodox, taken the huff. We had aln
written “got its knife into Coppin ”
as a matter of fact, the wrath,
or indignation (let us say) of the Age’s tv*’
prietor (which was presently aggravated bv°
large reduction in the Royal advertisement!
didn’t assume an aggressive form. His vo'.
of popular opinion merely remained dumb' 06
the subject of “ Dick Whittington,” whil s t°>
sang, often and loudly, the praises of rival
shows. It was especially unprejudiced i
puffing another pantomime (since deceased) at
the Alexandra, which, as attendances dwindled
down to the non-paying point, was found bv
the Age to “ show no indication of falling o ff j
popular favour,” and to “seem to improve h
dash and sparkle every week of its existence 11
when the imminent ending of said existence
was actually announced. Whether the Act
killed the one panto, with kindness whilst
strengthening the other with its neglect is a
possibility worth consideration. David Syme’s
contempt often acts as a tonic, and the breath
of his approval is apt to wither things right m
But this by the way.
» * *
The Age, which lately pleaded guilty to'tak-
ing a “ cursory glance ” at The Bulletin, re-
peated that indiscretion and so became aware
that the story of its quarrel with George
Ooppin was already going the round of Austra-
lia. The Age’s policy of silence was burst up, its
petty little hand exposed. With one eye (the
cursory eye) still glaring at The Bulletin and
the other gazing longingly on his ledger, David
Syme at last decided to sacrifice a few pounds
per week by refusing to accept any more advts,
from the Theatre Royal. Rash, petulant, short-
sighted person ! So long as he had taken the
advt. and said nothing about the show in
his gratuitous records of public amusements
David was simply suppressing a truth, a
very common occurrence in ‘ ‘ respectable ”
journalism. Pushing Dick, Tom, or Harry to
the fore, and keeping George covered up, as
you may say. But when he declined to take
the money, repudiating the glittering greed of
the Theatre Royal for two inches of advt. space
David was doing all that in him lay to deprive
a legitimate enterprise of its legitimate means
of support. Theatres, under present conditions
really buy the means of existence from news-
papers, and no expensive entertainment could
live without advertising. Mr. Syme has a sub-
lime belief in this doctrine, and members of his
staff, likely enough, echo the great master’s
sentiments when they amble around the press-
circle declaring that the Royal treasury is
losing £3OO a-week as the result of the Age’s
latest move. However, no such amount of
damage seems to have been inflicted upon the
theatre, although the colossal income of Syme
is evidently a decimal less than it might be.
# # #
In the long run, the Age, sticking to its
vengeance, would find much cause for regret,
inasmuch as the worthlessness of its theatrical
intelligence would pass into a proverb. But,
in the meantime, Mr. George Coffin has
to consider that, as competition goes on and
new things are produced, he couldn’t afford
to calmly ignore the fact that an influential
journal is studiously ignoring his theatre. An
established pantomime at the Xmas season is
almost independent of the Press, but a novelty
requires all the preliminary push it can get.
In plain English, if the Age persists in refusing
to advertise the Theatre Royal in the usual
way, the theatre must advertise itself at the
Age’s expense. Coffin has it in his power to
speak from the stage every evening—he once, it
may be remembered, spoke from the stage with
crushing effect about another Melbourne paper—
and printon the programmes a simple statement,
which would be worth a yard of space in Syme’s
paper. Such speeches from the stage are, in
the usual way, angry protests against fair treat-
ment, and go for nothing. Here would be an
exactly opposite case. Considering that at least
one other Melbourne manager has complained in
the past of unfair treatment from the Age, and
has discovered that its tactics of offence can’t be
quietly endured for ever, the wonder is that a
league of managers hasn’t sprung from the
present opportunity. Why don’t they all
quarrel with the Age, and save several hun-
dreds per annum apiece ? Do they suppose that
the flood of theatre-goers would be affected by
a cessation in the flow of Age notices, or by a
system of all-round “slating” in that one
particular journal ? Bah ! If three or
four theatres chose to pull together, not
necessarily as regards the length of their
advts., but in the choice of their adver-
tising-media, they would all stand the same
relative chances of success as at present, and all
effect the same percentage of saving on their
present expenses. Think over it. Four or
five theatres constitute a monopoly in either
Melbourne or Sydney, and can limit their own
advt. market just as easily as they can try to
cut one another’s throats. Is there no esprit da
corps among theatrical managers ?
PLAIN ENGLISH.
“Wanted—A Crown Defender.”
Dear Bulletin, —In last week’s issue you
published an article with the above heading iu
which you demonstrate the necessity for u
Crown Defender as the natural complement or
the Crown Prosecutor. 8o far the theory is a
good one, for when the Crown supplies an
officer to work up the case against a friendless
and penniless prisoner, it is absolutely fair tha
it should also supply a similar officer to work
up the case iu his favour—otherwise the jury
never gets a chance to hear the defence, an
the accused has no earthly show, whether
guilty or innocent. But the plan has one
great defect; it is incomplete. When the law
arrests a friendless and impecunious suspect--'
and the individuals on whom the law drops are
generally impecunious—it carefully puts him m
gaol, and then it sends out a corps if train 6
detectives to hunt up alhthe facts they can n®
to secure his conviction. They may distort tn
facts, or they may even invent them, or they


v find out Just enough of the truth to make
"blasting case against the prisoner and stop
fhere whereas a more complete investigation
nuld have put the matter in an entirely new
ivlit These officers supply the materials for
he Crown Prosecutor—and though a lawyer
1 aV at times, pull through without any materials
they are generally essential. Meanwhile the
1 risoncr, being placed carefully under lock, and
hev has no opportunity to hunt up facts and
witnesses on his own side. Even if he has
money the detectives are not at his service—
their business is to prove him guilty ; and if he
1 , (S 110 money neither the detectives nor any
other legal agencies whatever are at his service.
Therefore, in an intricate case the Crown
Prosecutor will be armed with all the evidence
which the force can rake together, while the
prown Defender, having no outside assistance,
will be left to paw the air and depend on
rhetoric and fireworks and perorations. In
many instances, such as that of Edward Bell,
in Melbourne, the police begin by getting on a
wrong track, and having found a prisoner, they
build up a case to fit him, and obstinately
refuse to consider even the possibility of his
innocence or of anybody else’s guilt. Under
SU ch conditions an innocent man can only save
himself by finding the guilty one through his
own efforts and at his own expense. If Bell
had been defended by a Crown official he would,
in all probability, have arrived in gaol just the
same, unless that official had been aided by
a detective department of his own, whose
duty it was to collate his case and
to hunt up all testimony favourable to the
prisoner. The evidence was readily avail-
able ; nearly every detective in the local force
was convinced of Bell’s innocence and of the
real offender’s guilt —save only the one officer
who worked up the charge. But the business of
the department was to secure convictions, not
to demonstrate anybody’s innocence ; there was
nobody to gather up the facts for the accused ;
and even had a Crown Defender been in
existence, there would have been no channel
through which these facts would ever have
reached him—unless, indeed, that dignified
barrister went forth in disguise and did his
own police duty, which is a highly improbable
circumstance. The Crown Defender, in fact,
under present circumstances, would be a species
of naked and unarmed legal ornament, without
even a brief to travel upon. It is absolutely
essential that such a functionary should be ap-
pointed, but along with him there must be
created a separate detective department to
prepare the case for the defence. A Defender
without any defence is hardly worth having. —
Yours, etc., Titus Salt.
Wanted—An Enquiry.
Judge Boucaut, of South Australia, the other
day, finished an inquiry into what is mildly
described as the “conduct” of James
Anderson, captain of the British ship Mill-
wall. Anderson’s “conduct,”’ as alleged
by the witnesses, consisted in. the fact
that he had banged and kicked his
second mate almost to death, while that
unfortunate officer was too ill to retaliate;
that he had removed him, when he was dying,
to a bunk in a deck-house, where—the weather
being rough—he was perpetually drenched
with water ; and that he had refused him even
the scanty medical comforts within reach—in
short, that the mate had been killed by brutal
violence, wanton exposure, and neglect. The
matter was carried, in the first place, before
the Marine Board, but that body decided that
it could not interfere unless in a case where the
captain had neglected his duty as a seaman,
and thereby endangered his vessel—in other
words, the Marine Board considered that its
business was to look after property, and that
human life was not its affair. Then a species
of informal inquiry was held before Judge
Boucaut. Two witnesses the carpenter
and a seaman swore that the mate’s
death was caused, or ■ at least acceler-
ated, by a process of slow brutality,
and no dispassionate evidence was called to dis-
prove their statement, yet on the strength of
the captain’s bald assertion Judge Boucaut
went out of his way to assert that lie utterly
disbelieved their testimony. He didn’t order
the rest of the crew to be called as witnesses,
but simply let the matter drop there and then,
and he rashly gave some of his reasons for
doing so, which were about the most surprising
reasons ever heard in a court of justice. For
instance :
The captain may, or may not, have struck the second
mate, and if he did it was a wrong thing for the captain
to do, but he did not call upon the captain to answer
that. . . Supposing the kicking did take place it was
without doubt a wrong thing to do, but the mate could
have had the matter investigated before a magistrate at
one of the ports, and be could have been fined for it.
In other words, the Judge apparently didn’t
believe the mate had been ill-treated because
he didn’t lay a complaint and “have the matter
investigated.” And the trifling fact that the
mate was dead before reaching port, and past
laying complaints, didn’t seem to count at all.
Again, when a witness swore that the captain
was “rolling about and smelt of liquor,” the
Judge, as reported, almost got into a rage at
this aspersion on that officer’s character ;—-
-AVhat! Do you mean to say that, with a gale of wind
plowing, you could smell the captain’s breath. The thing
is absorb, that’s the sort of thing they tell in America,
tou almost make me angry.
Buch a Judge as that is too innocent for this
world. Anyone who stands to leeward of an
individual who has got drunk on ship’s rum
can smell him in a hurricane. Vet Boucaut dis
bussed the case, apparently for the two reasons
-hat the mate didn’t lay an information, after
16 was dead, and that a drunken man can’t be
smelt in a gale, therefore a witness who pro-
fessed to smell him must be utterly unreliable.
At the same time lie refused to allow costs,
and ] ie didn’t order the arrest of the two
witnesses, which was an absolutely unaccount-
a . omission, for either the captain had com-
mitted murder or his accusers had committed
perjury, and Judge Boucaut was thus tacitly
" inking at one out of two utterly horrible
crimes. Evidently, in Boucaut’s estimation,
muucide at sea, unlike homicide on land, is a
Hnu dl affair, and hardly worth noticing.
I hen any one is accused of flic latter crime,
he authorities raise hea’eu and earth to find
every witness who can cast any light upon the

swore that an offence had been committed and
the accused said it hadn’t, and no one even
thought it worth while to fetch the rest of the
crew into court to give their version of the
affair.

Plural Voting in N.S. Wales: Its Origin.
The New South Wales plural vote emanated
from the brains of the subtle politicians whose
prestige was endangered by the introduction of
manhood suffrage. It is an excrescence on the
original Constitution. Its history forms a large
chapter in the career of the very Premier
Parkes who was directed last June by the
vox populi to re-enact single voting. For it is
beyond dispute that Parkes, both by vote and
voice, assisted to barter away the liberties of
the people. He assisted to give real estate a
co-equal voice with manhood in the Councils of
the province. The Constitution, for the first
few years of its existence, provided for single
voting ; but this principle was quietly dropped
when manhood suffrage was made an electoral
qualification in 1858. The original qualification
of an elector was the possession of a freehold of
the value of £lOO, or leasehold of the annual
value of £lO, or the occupancy of a building
of the annual value of £lO, or the holding
of a pastoral license, or the receipt of an
annual salary of £lOO, or the rental of a
room in a lodging-house. No man could
be registered unless he were a resident of
six months’ standing in a district ; and
no voter could poll until all rates and taxes, if
any, had been paid. Plural voting, therefore,
was not allowable under the Constitution of
New South Wales in the first instance any
more than it is allowable under the Constitu-
tion of South Australia at the present time.
Single voting in New South Wales received its
death-blow from the hand of “Slippery
Charlie ” Cowper, with the approbation of
Robertson, Deniehy, Dalley, Parkes, Pid-
dington, Flood, Forster, Thornton, and
Byrnes. Parkes and Thornton alone survive
in the Legislature. The original Constitution
was shorn of the electoral sections ; and in a
new Electoral Act the parliamentary franchise
was given to every adult male resident of six
months’ standing, and, without regard to such
residence, to, amongst others, every subject
having a freehold of the clear unencumbered value
of £lOO or a leasehold of clear unencumbered
value of £lO per year or a pastoral lease or
license from the Crown. The balance of voting
power was vested in the owners and lessees
of real estate, in club-men, and in occu-
pants of offices. An elector qualified by man-
hood could vote only in his own electorate;
but an elector of the non-resident class could
vote in every electorate in which he held a
qualification ; and if he happened to be doubly
qualified for a district, he was restricted to one
vote for that district. The new qualifications
were debated at much length ; but the principle
of plural voting emerged safely from the ordeal.
The final division on the clause throws a side-
light on the conduct of Parkes. Will it be
believed that Parkes helped to “ tell” the ayes
in that memorable division ? Plural voting
plus manhood suffrage was substituted for
single voting by the votes of Parkes, Cowper,
Robertson, Martin, Thornton and Byrnes.
And, by a strange irony of fate, Parkes was
the first Premier to rise in the Assembly and
move the abolition of plural voting ! His
sincerity was suspected, and the doubt was
amply justified by his subsequent conduct. Has
Parkes ever been an honest advocate of man-
hood suffrage ?

Boiling a Family.
Last week, in South Melbourne, a gentleman
named Cobb arrived home in a rage, thrashed
his wife, broke the furniture, kicked one of his
sons, and made a great effort to choke another
one. Then he knocked down his wife and the
two youths, and deliberately poured boiling
water over them. Mrs. Cobb is horribly dis-
figured for life as the result of this episode, and
the two sons are both frightfully injured. On
Saturday the law crashed heavily down on Mr.
Cobb on account of these proceedings, and told
him thathe was a fiend and a monster of iniquity.
Then it sent him to gaol for a whole six months,
and the papers mention, as an evidence that
the criminal code is an awful thing when
roused, that this was done “ without the option
of a fine.” If Cobb, instead of boiling three
people, had stolen £2O or so, he would probably
have got three years, and to a dead certainty
he would have got 12 months ; and it would
have been entirely superfluous to mention that
he wasn’t allowed the option of a fine. The
code regards boiling three people as a mere
trifling unpleasantness compared with a
deficiency in the cash ; it heaves awful
language at Cobb and tells him that
he is a loathsome wretch and utterly unfit
to live, and announces that it will fall on him
in a way that will strike terror into all such
individuals for the future, and then it gives
him just half a year : and, at the same time, it
tells the small embezzler that it will let him
down lightly on account of his youth and pre-
vious good character, and gives him, probably,
eighteen months. In other words, the law and
its officers are so utterly callous, that they
regard six months’ detention in a commodious
and airy gaol as a fearful vengeance upon the man
who only boiled his family, and consider three
times that punishment as a light and mitigated
penalty on the person who bolts with a small
cheque. And even in its brutal disregard for
life and its grovelling worship of property the
South Melbourne bench is utterly illogical.
Mrs. Cobb, in her natural or unboiled state,
had a certain market value as a worker or
animal of burden ; she will probably be incapa-
citated or her value greatly diminished for at
least three months, and allowing only 15s.
a-week as the worth of her services, the loss in
hard cash is £9 15s. Estimating the falling-off
in the value of her two sons at 10s. a-week
eacli for the same period, there is a further loss
of £l3, "or a total of £22 15s. It is a very low
calculation indeed to put down medical
expenses for a whole scalded family at £l5,
making a grand total—irrespective of smashed
furniture and damage to clothes—of £37 15s.
If Alexander Cobb, being a man with no
influential friends, had embezzled that amount
in the ordinary way, without boiling water or
other violence, he would have got at least a
year in gaol —many a man, for no greater an
injury to the sacred interests of property, has
got ten years, but we put the sentence down at
the lowest estimate. Therefore, Mr. Cobb gets
a reduction of at least six months—or, it may
be, as much as nine years and six months—
because he boiled his family in addition to his
offence against property ; in fact, in the eye of
the law it is plainly a commendable thing to
boil a family.
A week or so ago the magistrates at Melbourne
City Court tried one David Byrne for stealing
a shilling, and sent him to gaol for three months.
This is at the rate of exactly fourpence a month,
and on the same principle two big embezzlers
recently sentenced would have been sent up for
570,000 years and 226,000 years respectively,
while Fourdrinier, who was, comparatively
speaking, only a small shortage man, would
have been locked up for a little over seventy
centuries. It would be interesting to know on
what principle—if there really is a principle and
the Law doesn’t toss up —sentences are inflicted.
Either the penalty is supposed to be dealt out
according to the amount of damage done by
the defendant to the plaintiff, or else it is
measured by the moral aspect of the case
as a breakage of the Ten Commandments,
or else, again, the Court may make an effort to
combine the two considerations, which is much
the same as trying to add a mile-and-a-half to
£1 3s. 9d. In the first case there is a manifest
absurdity in sending David Byrne to gaol for
three months for the theft of a shilling while
Fourdrinier gets only 12 months for a dis-
crepancy of over £l4OO, and if the law considers
only the damage done, either Byrne’s sentence
is utterly brutal and atrocious, or else the
Courts have shown gross partiality to Four-
drinier, either from ignorance or some other
cause not specified. Again, if the brief, tran-
sitory Panton of the period tries to fix his
sentences on the moral aspect of the offence, then
there is probably little difference between the
breakage of one commandment and thebreakage of
another—there is a smashed commandment, in
either case—and everybody ought to get the
same penalty. Apparently, however, he endea-
vours, in some unaccountable fashion, to combine
the two elements, and to maintain, at the same
time, a due regard for the accused person’s
respectability or the reverse ; he multiplies the
cash stolen by the broken commandment, and
divides the result by the prisoner’s clothes and
bell-topper, and the final product is the sen-
tence. And as the average “ beak ” is a
hideously bad arithmetician, and has nobody to
audit his accounts, the results arrived at are
eminently surprising.

When Rudyard Kipling was asked what
was his first impression on landing in Sydney, he
replied, “ The loose flagstones which when trod on
squirt dirty water over the unfortunate
pedestrian.”
“ Colonial English,” a collection of Aus-
tralian and Anglo-Indian Argot by Karl Lentz-
ner, Ph. Dr., who describes himself on his title-
page, “formerly examiner in modern languages
at the Sydney Grammar School and at the King’s
School, Parramatta,” was published at the end of
last year at London, and almost instantly with-
drawn. The latter step was the proper one to
take, and the former was very improper, as the
material, badly compiled at the best, violated
several copyrights. The illustrations given of
Australian slang are very funny for the most
part, and where they are exact and authentic the
source, in nine instances out of ten, is inaccurately
stated. The very faults of the work, however,
will give it a value in collectors’ eyes, and those
who were fortunate enough to get hold of one of
the few copies which reached Australia may now
be interested in learning that the book is already
selling at fancy prices.
In the by-gone days—somewhere about
the seventies—“ The Vagabond ” once undertook,
in a rash moment, to deliver a lecture on the
Chinese question in Brisbane. Julian was not a
good speaker then—he is nob a first-rate one now,
but 20 years ago he was almost as bad as Stanley
—and the audience did not take him kindly. Still
he struggled along till a big German became abso-
lutely insupportable, and then the“Vag.” appealed
to him in pathetic terms: “Now, my Teutonic
friend,” he said, “do be calm.” The Rhinelander
didn’t become calm; in fact he grew more
vociferous, and presently Julian tried again. “I
would appeal to my Teutonic friend,” he said
kindly, “not to interrupt.” More howls, while
“the Vag.” struggled to bear up against the
storm. “If my Teutonic friend,” he began again,
in despair, and’ then the German leaped on a form
and cursed in real earnest. “ Tonigs,” he
shrieked ; “ Tonigs, I vos not von vot brinks your
soft tacks. I vash not brink tonigs. Yen I brinks
I trinks goot prandy.” Julian gathered up his
sermon then and left, and the German took
possession of the hall and lectured all by himself
on drinks in general.
Mr. Frank Rogers, Q.C., now President
of N.S.W. Land Court, with a salary of £l5OO,
is to be raised to the rank of a Supreme Court
Judge, with a salary of £2600, and is to fill in
his spare time in hearing non-Land-Court cases.
Mr. Rogers is not only a good advocate, but a
most able lawyer. However, he doesn’t look like a
lawyer, but like a low-comedian, his countenance
being decorated with a lugubrious smile, appar-
ently copied from a’mask of Comedy which we
once saw on an actor’s tombstone in a Parisian
cemetery. During the trial of “ Captain
Moonlight,” and while that fine ruffian was
addressing the jury, Mr. Rogers, who was sitting
at the court table, turned his head for a moment
to speak to a man beside him, whereupon “ Moon-
light” turned fiercely upon the Q.C. and accused
him of jeering at his misfortunes—at the misfor-
tunes of a man fighting against odds for his life
(“Moonlight” was hanged as a consequence of
the trial). Now, Mr. Rogers, who is a most sym-
pathetic man, was doing nothing of the. kind, and
arose and apologetically told the prisoner so. It
was poor Rogers’ smile that had got him into all
the trouble.
In a Front Pew.
I wonder if anyone will dare to remark upon my
coming so early. If they do, I shall not care. I
have gained my point. I have a good seat here,
right at the back of the pews reserved for the
family; and I can see everything. I’m close
enough to the chancel to note every wave of feel-
ing that crosses his face ! and he has such a tell-
tale face. I’ve watched it many a time at closer
range than this. I wonder whether —but no ! I
didn’t come here to wonder. I’m not going to
grow sentimental. I made up my mind to be an
iceberg; to be coldly critical; to smile a cynical
smile, even while my heart bleeds. O, I hope no
one will say ill-natured things because I came
early. I really do care a great deal. If they
were to say that the girl he jilted was at the
church door before it was open because she still
loves him, I should simply go mad. I wish
I had not come so early, or laid myself open
to such remarks. If I had stopped away perhaps
it would have better indicated my indifference.
But, no. Then they would have said I was at
home, crying my eyes out because of my lost love.
After all, who knows that I was the first one in
the church? No one, except the groomsmen, and
men are not gossips. If they were women I should
have reason to feel uneasy.
It is as I supposed. They have used chrysan-
themums, and chrysanthemums only, in the
decoration. White and yellow. More yellow
than white, too. Ah ! yes. Yellow’s for jealousy.
But, then, there are more leaves than flowers.
More green than yellow. How very appropriate !
Green signifies fickleness ; and there’s not a sprig
of blue in all the sanctuary. Blue’s for constancy.
And I What made me do it ? How awfully
thoughtless of me ! —I have on a blue gown. Will
they comment on that, I wonder ?
I was not so very much ahead of time. There
are fully a score of people scattered about the
pews now, and the groomsmen are growing busier
every minute. Ido not propose to move up for
anyone. I must have this corner seat. I came to
see, and I’m going to see. Here come those
dreadful relations of hers, the Parvenus. I never
could understand how he could get over that
stumbling-block. Fancy marrying into the family
of a man who cut off lamb-chops in the market for
your mother when she first began housekeeping !
Papa says that if men got their deserts in this
world, Parvenu would be in prison.
And there are her cousins, the O’Gradys ! Who
was it told me that Jim O’Grady was the champion
professional middle-weight prize-fighter of Bir-
mingham five years ago, before his father made
two millions and a-half in mining stocks and then
died and left it to his three sons and two
daughters ?
Gracious goodness ! What a scowl Dick
Flatterby has on ! How is it men can’t hide their
feelings ? Everybody knows he was hot foot after
the bride of to-day, before to-day’s bridegroom
stepped in and carried her heart by storm. I
hope I’m not scowling like that. Why, the man
actually carries his heart on his sleeve. If I were
in his place, I think I should have been too busy
to come. A man has so many excuses for stopping
away; whereas a woman —heavens ! How ill-
natured I am !
There’s the organ, at last. I wondered whether
we were ever going to have any music. She doesn’t
know a note, and I’d begun to think that possibly
out of deference to her ignorance they had decided
to disnense with the usual preliminary recital.
Can it be that he is playing Schumann’s
“Reverie?” O, how cruel ! How cruel! Why,
I have played that for him myself, a hundred
times. I played it the last—Come ! Come ! This
will never do. Why, everything is getting hazy.
My eyes are wet, I know ; and—O, if anyone
should see me use a handkerchief, what would
they say ?
There! I hope no one noticed it. Here come
his mother and sister. Let me see ! Three minutes
of the time. In a quarter of an hour they will be
her mother and her sister, and it is less than three
months since I was sure that one day they would
be mine. Oh ! how I should have loved that dear,
stately old lady, with her white hair ! and how
fond I should have been of that girl, so
How I hate them both ! How I hate him, and
everything that belongs to him ! I shall be able
to show them some day, I hope. Oh, yes ! That
is why I am here; because I hate him, and not
because, as some people may think, there is a
spark of the old love still left.
It is already one minute past the time. Every-
body is looking around. Everybody in the church,
I think, except myself. I will not look. Now the
organ has stopped. Ah ! the bridal party has
arrived. Now they have rushed at double-quick
time to the doorway ; and —yes, yes. It is he. I
saw his face there, just for a second, peeping out
from the vestry. I wonder if he is nervous.
Suppose he should refuse to marry her at the last
minute?
Heaven ! how that organist is bungling the
wedding-march ! I don’t believe he could ever
have played it before. They are coming up the
aisle now, I know, though I will not look around.
It is solemn, anybody must say it is. That slow,
measured step: it is like a walk to the execu-
tioner’s block. Here he comes. Her brother is
his best man, and how small, how insignificantly
small he looks beside the bridegroom, beside you.
I have ray eyes upon your face now. Do you
feel them? Do you? You do, I am sure; and
yet you dare not look. You are very graceful,
and yet I have seen you offer your arm with more
grace than you do to your bride. You have
offered it to me—and your smile, oh it is a
ghastly smile, sir. ... I didn’t think your
voice 'would tremble so, I thought you were
stronger than that. How glad lam ! You feel
my eyes upon you still, don’t you ? Do you feel
the hate that is in them? It is the hate that is
choking you. How proud she must be of you,
stuttering and stammering there beside her. I
almost feel sorry for her. . . .
It is over—over at last—and you are hers. Her
husband—not mine. She is your wife. Will you
look at me, I wonder ? You must pass so close to
me. Will you look? Will you? Will you? You
must! I will make you ! You shall see the hate
that is in my heart. You shall! You shall !
My God ! I made him! He looked at me
fully and squarely. His eyes—his dear eyes—and
they told me so much ! While in mine—no ; there
was not hate—there was love, love , love !—Haw k.
Dear Bulletin,—! lately r.eacl a story in
a Sydney paper about Australian wines being sold
in London as French. This is a fiction, for, to
begin with, people don’t often pawn off a superior
article as an inferior one, and at present, at least,
Australian wines cannot compare with the French
—that is real French wines, not the manufacture
of Cette and Hamburg. One often ]tears these
stories of French connoisseurs being taken in, but
they are pure inventions. As a matter of fact all
the wine you can produce for export can be sold
here for what it is. One firm alone sells more
Australian wine in England than is exported from
the Australias, and Italy has to be largely drawn
upon to make up the deficit. —L., Loudon, Ist
December, 1891.

My Dear Moorabinda,—
Improvements to the members’ stand atßandwiok were
foreshadowed by Mr. Dangar at the recent A.J.C. inert,
ing, but what shape they are to take will remain a deep
architectural mystery until society is serenely sitting on
the inevitable wet paint. But I fervently hope that
when they have gilded and glorified the southern end of
Ihe grand-stand to transfoimation-scene pitch they will
erect automatic barriers which will keep everybody out
but people who have a right there, and so do away with
those unedifying man-chases which happen before nearly
every race. And, talking of Randwick improvements, a
carriage-shelter is shrieked for by people whose vehicles
crack and blister in the fervid sun every fine race-day,
hut the committee listen coyly to the prayer.
Talking indirectly about horses, what does the published
name-list of 126 Sydney equestrians under the introduc-
tion “ the following amateur riders har e been registered”
—mean ? Can it signify that these local Mazeppas—some
of whom are well-known in sassiety—want to go in for the
professional jacket and cap ? And if not, what do they
want to be registered for—like poodles in the Spring ?
Rev. Grubb has shaken the [dust of Sydney' from his
feet after having fought a great fight. The elect left
behind now hold meetings which ail those spiritually
stirred up by the prophet are cordially invited to attend.
But the meeting that ensnares my patronage will be the
mass meeting of sufferers through landladies, servants,
wives, and housekeepers generally rushing off to Grubb
and thereby sowing the seeds of hunger and despair in
many a happy home. For instance, one high priestess of
rechauffe got Grubb very severely, and on the day of the
dear man’s last stance she dropped in casually at the
various business addresses of her boarders and told them
that as she and her daughters were going to the last
appearance of the disciple, he (boarder) would have to
forage round for his own tea when he got home. She had
left the cold mutton where the cat really couldn’t reach
it without getting on the table, and she was sure Mr.
Blank would make himself comfortable, &e. And then
there was the other hash-house Grubb-worshipper who
politely asked her gentlemen if they “ could be home to
tea by five o’clock?—the meetings begin so early.”
The first day of the cavalry tournament on flower-bed
decorated Rosehill racecourse was on 22nd inst., but,
owing to the funeral of the late Sir John Hay happening
the same afternoon, many “ representative ” persons were
unavoidably not there, and their female relatives largely
went a-missing also. Lord Jersey rose not in the East
either, but H.E. was represented in a ddgag6, off-the-
chain sort of way by Goschen (victor in the polo event)
and Chumley, who, by the way, was the chameleon of the
day, so far as changes of free-and-easy raiment went.
“’Ow the Guv’ner’s young men do enjoy theirselves when
’is Hexcellency isn’t ’ere,” said someone behind me. But
other people’s attire was more orthodox. The Throck-
morton girls looked cool in white muslin frocks, with
white kid shoon en suite ; Miss Dangar wore black silk,
with a black hat larded with chiffon ; Mrs. Wrench in
figured electric-blue : Mrs. Spalding in pink ; Miss Spald-
ing also in the hue of the plaintive water-melon. Another
pretty girl was in pink muslin, spotted over with white, and a
flat black hat with pink aggravations, but somebody with
a face the hue of a new half-sovereign had recklessly got
itself up in Australian blue. Of course, the effect was
like the golden moon rising over a forget-me-not bush.

outbreak at Rosehill racecourse was a V.R. effervescence
with Gov. Jersey (he had substituted a black necktie for
his usual blue slip-knot, by which the crowd knew he was
mourning for the late Prince per medium of his habili-
ments) in a square, flower-hung box in the centre of the
stand. And just here, on this very ink-spot, I want to say
that enthusiastic committees should wrestle with the mad
longing to frame their beloved Quv’nor in green stuff.
The smell of various pungent vegetables giving up the
ghost in the sun has made more than one vice-regal
woman sorry hubby was so popular, for there are half-a-
dozen headaches and a nerve-crisis hidden in one cluster
of withering ferns for some people. There was no vice-
regal lady at Rosehill, of course, but Lady Charles Scott
with Mrs. M‘Neil and Miss Ryan were there, and
would have lit up the scene had they not been
wearing half-mast toilets. Lady Scott was in black
with a little silver braid about it, wide black hat
trimmed with chiffon; her sister in a white gown
with blouse spotted in black, white pique jacket and
black hat with coronet of violets ; Mrs. Mac Neil, In white
embroidered mull muslin with scarf of black chiffon and
black hat with chiffon bows—all very correct, but too
etohy in effect to get there chromatically. Ancrum,
Goschen, and Chumley squired the Governor’s party,
which was under the personal aegis of Loigi Precedence
Scott in a mourning suit of black and grey, betting-man
checks and a set of black studs, looking like inky tears of
woe that had congealed on his dickey. There was an
enormous frothy crowd on the grand-stand, but none of
the garments exactly soothed my savage breast. All the
pretty frocks seemed to have missed all the pretty women,
and I am not going to rage like a molar-toothache over
the beauties of a moire antique on a kite-shaped female
who would manage to look ill aD ease and oppressed by
her dressing if she simply had on the woven apron of
Ujiji belledom.
In the field, matters were lively on both days, and the
Judges must have had a trying time, for, to the lay
observer, the contests seemed very equal. The Victorian
militaires were gorgeous examples of the quantity of gold-
cord one man can carry without hurting himself, and
wfiy an eagleAould have looked one of them straight in
tlie uniform without gffiuUbliiTcP— but the new array of
the N.S.W. defenders, russet-brown with a deep V of red
in front, struck me as being an unhealthy design for any
soldier. The glaring frontal contrast seemed to me
nothing more or less than a pointer as to the whereabouts
of tlie warrior’s vital parts—but then, I know T so little
about soldiering, and the defenders of N.S.W. perhaps
mean not to get hit in front, so possibly it doesn’t
matter a hit. The greased men wrestling on horse-back
took an encore—and apropos of these, a myopic young-
lady, sitting out the show with a male friend, watched
these basted youths trying to pull one another off per
medium of any hold they could get, and when one caught
the other by the top of his continuations, she cried,
“Oh ! now he has him by the neck, hasn’t he?” And the
Johnny replied, hesitatingly, •“ Er—yes—that is by the
neck of his trousers.”
If temperance folk want more men to embrace ginger-
pop principles, why do they let fierce, advanced females
pervade all their little sprees and mob the festive teapot
at the rate of two to one? At those naughty public
affairs where “hard tack ” is introduced ladies are gently
relegated to the mat, or the galleries, hut directly a cup
of tea steams in the offing there ensues a mad rush of
women, and each male (I don’t call the tame curates
males) feels as if the cold hand of his molher-in-law had
him by the nape of the neck. Then wild and aggressive
females wag lantern jaws over the muffins until the flies
fall dizzily into the oleomargarine. It is a great pity the
local optionists don’t “ shoo ” their lady disciples away

A Hobart correspondent tells me that the leader of the
Tasmanian Opposition and his wife wanted to give a select
garden-party to the Science Congress, hut when the day
arrived everybody rushed the affair ; notwithstanding,
Mrs. Dobson received them all as if they had been her
dearest friends. lam also told that Tasmanian society is
a-weary of a sacred lady whose latest freak is to refuse to
wear evening dress on a very special occasion. However,
let the Tasmanians forgive the poor thing ; perhaps she
has a backbone like a string of cotton-reels.
The first private assembly ball of the season at Hobart
was a disappointing affair to many, because the CuraQoa
officers and many other visitors danced with Melbourne
and Sydney girls all through the night. The pretty Tas-
manians (those that are pretty, and some are not ) are
very much disgusted at this and vow retaliation ; still, as
several of them have run the show for many long years,
it is about time they made way for others who are not yet
in the sere and yellow leaf. Of course, we know that —
“ Beauty soon will pass away ;
Virtue will for ever stay.”
Still, you can’t get thoughtless, good-looking young
fellows to see the thing in that light.
Mayor Manning has grasped the simple licensed
victualler-ish art of entertaining the sporting visitor.
When the pioneers of the Victorian Eleven recently
arrived, His Worship received them at the Town
Hall with the usual expressions of rapt ecstacy, and then
herded his guests into the big hall to hear the organ—at
which you could see the “ongwee ” oozing out of the bat-
and-ball artists. However, the good Mayor knew his
men, and so, ere Wiegand had settled to his stride, His
Worship rose solemnly and led the whole drooping gang
into the blissful regions where the fizz is ever bubbling
and the corkscrew does not rest. None of the visitors
found their way hack again to the music.

luggage to the van the other day, it’s just probable that
the thing may be getting a little over-done.

They dance all through the summer in Maoriland, and
the local bank-clerk sometimes goes barn-darncing in
clothing that is too tight for him. A correspondent tells
me of a young man whose, that is to say, bifurcations
gave way in the shadow of the swallow tails—such a
carpet-baggy crack that the only thing left was to sit
down suddenly on the nearest bench and wait until the
guests had had enough. But the inevitable girl lay in
ambush to make him wish that the earth would open up
and swallow him, and he hadn’t sat long on fils widening
rent when a flushed damsel arose and with a lovely smile
asked him if he would be so kind as to give her her hand-
kerchief—but really—if he would excuse her—and with-
out further warning she pounced upon a piece of white
rag which might have been her mouchoir, only that ball
handkerchiefs don’t as a general thing pay out for over a
yard when you hang on to one of them. And then the
frantic victim arose with a bloodthirsty expression in his
eye that belied the flag of truce at the rear, and fled
head-first for the dressing-room.
England gives orders that colonial military officers are
to be eligible for positions in the Imperial army. Per the
usual high wire conditions, of course—the candidates to
be nominated by the handiest Governor, which means
that the Australian Yon Moltke in embryo must have had
lucky forebears who struck it rich in the days of—er—
Captain Cook if he wants to air his tactics in Her
Majesty’s onety-oneth. This ukase has been joyously
received by sassiety, and many ambitious local militaires
over the age limit (22) harbour serious thoughts of post-
dating their baptismal certificates. On dit that a young
officer—heir to a big wool-business—will be the first to
travel Bullwards after a commission.

Five inspectors of N.S.W. agriculture
have been appointed at =£3oo each. From all we
hear concerning a couple of these officials, they
know as much about the subject as did the Mark
Twain agricultural editor, who recommended that
turnips, instead of being pulled, should be simply
shaken from the trees.
Abraham Friedman, one of Sydney’s best-
advertised usurers, has formally become bank-
rupt. “He who lives by the sword shall perish
by the sword.” Abraham, “ who was worth
£250,000 three years ago,” must have been specu-
lating instead of investing—must, in a weak
moment, have backed a bill-—must have lent
people money without security or listened to some
mining stories. If so, however, he errs in historical
company. Even Shylock allowed sentiment to
overcome him.
Mr. John Lackey succeeds Sir John Hay
as President of N.S.W. Legislative Council, thus
quenching the hopes of the N.S.W. Opposition,
which thought to see that 41 political pugilist,”
Edmund Barton, desert the Ministry. By the
way, the Council presidentship is now in the gift
of the Ministry, and a bill originating in the
Council and conferring the right to elect its own
president is now before that body. It is exceed-
ingly unlikely that anything enlarging the
privileges of the Council will be done by the
Assembly.
Certain obsequious N.S.W. railway officials
who, no doubt scenting favours to come, recently
sought to raise a departmental testimonial
emphasising the beauty of the character of one of
the junior “bosses” (salary over .£500) were
cordially jumped upon by Chief Commissioner
Eddy, who sensibly ukases that all testimonials
must be postponed until the beneficiary-elect
leaves the service. This will stop the system of
phlebotomy to which the obscure and unin-
fluential railway-servant has so often had to submit
in silent helplessness.
The dull, horrid monotony of sweltering
Townsville has been broken up somewhat this week.
For a day or two there were ten separate rivers
rushing through the town, and almost the whole of
ten or twelve bye-streets were washed into the
main thoroughfare, and the main thoroughfare
was mostly washed into the sea. Nearly three-
quarters of a yard of rain came down inside 48
hours, and the rest of the yard looked, upon latest
advices, as if it might arrive at any moment. The
Catholic Church fell in, and a lot of houses melted
away, also one bridge, a lighter, a few hundred
yards of pavement, and other sundries. The
population is hard at work shovelling frogs, eels,
and slush off the roofs, and scaring the crabs off
the chimney tops, and removing the vegetation
and dead cats that are impaled on the lightning-
rod. One or two inhabitants are unaccounted
for, all the pianos are rusted, every small boy in
the town has ruined his pants by floating around
on a plank with nails in it, everybody has got his
feet wet and is troubled with prawns in his boots,
hardly anybody has had a square meal for three
days, because it is necessary to dive for the coal,
the gospel-shops won’t be tenantable for three
months, owing to the horned cattle that took
refuge there, and other troubles are too numerous
to mention.
Editor Bulletin. Sir, — In re your para-
graph of last week, let me say that the yacht
Wanderer, as a matter of fact, never reached
Sydney on her last voyage to Australia, but was
wrecked at Port Macquarie when attempting to
enter in 1851, in charge of the master of the
schooner Elizabeth Cohen (G. R. Easton), then
lying in port, who was induced to try and get her
in although she drew some feet more water than
there was on the bar. The master of the Wan-
derer (an American, named Hathaway) gave out
that Mr. Boyd (the owner) had gone ashore at
San Christoval with his gun and accompanied by
a kanaka boy, and had not been afterwards seen.
There was something very shady and mysterious
about tbe whole affair, but it seemed to be
nobody’s business at the time to initiate enquiry,
and so the matter of Mr. Boyd’s fate was never
thoroughly sifted. The probabilities are that the
facts of the case will never be known.— Truth.
Port Macquarie, 24th Jan., 1892.
It is most convenient to have a first-class
room near tram stopping places, where ladies
can rest themselves, and if they choose, have a
good cup of tea, or other refreshments. We
understand the Country Milk Co.’s “ Cascade,”
80 and 82 Elizabeth-street, near King-street, is just
the place; it is certainly the largest and best-
conducted room in Sydney, and is getting quite
the fashionable resort for afternoon tea. We also
understand that ladies who so desire can have
parcels sent there, so that they can call for them
just before taking the tram for home.*
To the inventive genius of a lady in 1843
are we indebted for the first ice-cream freezer,
but those ladies who wish to save time, labour,
and money invest in the new German Freezer.
Why ? There is no handle to turn, not half so
much ice used, and it keeps the cream firm in or
out doors. Two quarts of ice cream can be made
for Is. 6d. to Is. 9d. Send for Sydney testimonials
to T. A. Knight & Co., St. James Chambers,
King-street, Sydney, who supply the machine.
Price, 305., with instructions so simple that a
child can understand.

“ Uncles and Aunts,” a farcical coxaedv
three acts, by W. Lestocq and Walter Everard
was produced for the first time in Sydney h!5,7
a crowded audience on Saturday last. This piece
belongs to the man-trap and mistaken- den itv
brand of protracted foolery, and consists m’ S
of an antique beau, and an assortment of elder) e
people who stand in the relation of guardt-m/ n
an assortment of youngish folk, who pervade the
scene with courtship and juvenile idiocy general
There is practical y no plot, and all the humour of
the piece is supplied by the fact that somebody
always mistaken for somebody else who m e,,
appears to be nobody in particular. The
ment is briefly this : a young girl loves a young
man, but her aunt refuses to receive him been !
Ins uncle withholds his consent to the union Thu
young girl (Mary Harley) is m despair,' when
assistance comes in the guise of an antiquated
suitor sent to her by her father. With her couste
Nelly Harley s aid, she persuades the old dandy
to personate her lover’s stern uncle; and thus the
ball of trouble is set a-rolhng. After this initial
absurdity it becomes impossible to trace the com
plicated entanglements, and the piece bounds
along for three acts amid a chaos of impossible
situations and improbable gyrations.

Pantomime still reigns at the Royal, but
theie is already an appreciable dropping off in
the attendance ; the glitter and glory of mere
spectacle is beginning to nail upon the public-
palate, and the gods are giving but jaded attention
to the whistling song of liose Dearing, or the
dainty dancing of the Hairy Hour. The Crage
Family are yet able to excite enthusiasm, but
there must be a limit to the perpetuity of motion
of even an acrobatic lineage. Hence the “Forty
Ihieves will shortly make their last raid, and
an Italian Opera Company will impinge upon the
P* a y‘£oer s horizon, these foreign minstrels in
turn to be succeeded by the ever-bland Holt with
a melodrama with many hundred acts, and several
acres of limelight and Union Jacks.

Moore in the Wood,” and the babes who ought
n to have been in it on any account; and to
night (Saturday ) a comedy-drama entitled “Forty-

man that was hanged the day before he was to
have been married, we hope that it will all turn
out for the best, and that nobody will be dis
appointed. We are getting tired of pantomime,
anyway.

The Polytechnic is growing in public
favour as the one place of amusement where the
jaded soul can indulge the luxury of tobacco while
it contemplates the mystery of clog-darming, and
solaces itself with the spectacle of twenty alleged
American lady minstrels. Here, too, Helix Tanner
has been delighting the philosophic mind by sitting
at a table and reading on an empty stomach, and
maintaining a reasonable degree of plumpness on
the succulent desert air, but he had to leave it oft
suddenly as starvation is getting so common in
Australia that nobody would roll up to look at
him.

“Much Ado about Nothing’' is going
downhill steadily at Melbourne Bijou. The
departure of Messrs. Brough and Tom Cannam
for Sydney necessitated two changes from the
original cast. Clitheroe verges nearer to dull
inanity than his predecessor, who was by no means
a successful realisation of Dogberry’s doddering
colleague, whilst James Cathoart is a stagey,
mechanical Leonato, where Brough was unusually
easy and natural. Brough walked leisurely, yet
impressively, through the part, and the veteran
,faines does it on the old “three dots and carry
ime” system. Of late, too, Percy Lyndal has put
ho much vigour into his delivery that one would as
lief hear the town crier speak his lines. It is at
the end of the second act that Percy shows most
anxiety to shout for the crowd. Being unable to
bring the curtain down on a situation (because the
Bard hasn’t provided one) he opens bis shoulders
at the upper circle, and endeavours to frighten
’em into a conviction that he’s saying something
tremendous. “ We are the only love gods ” comes
from that amiable young man’s mouth like a dis-
charge of six minute guns, each gun being more
loaded than the one before. Dash it all ! can’t
he adore the gods without trying to split their
ear-drums ?
* * * *
“ Dick Whittington ” pulled through the
sultry tug-of-war week better than the other
theatres, and had a big crowd there on Saturday
night, close upon the heels of a matinee.
Ureville’s animated bustle is quite a novel feature
of the pantomime, now that an exactly similar
piece of wholesome fun is no longer perpetrated
at the x\leck. The hinder part of Greville’s ward-
robe contains a boy, and becomes detached from
the rest of the female’s person after a while,
whereupon J.R.G. whistles to his rear-guard, and
it follows him off like a dawg. In the event of
this hidden urchin becoming a great actor, he will
look back with pride to the time when he made
his first appearance on the stage in the small, but
important, character of a dress-improver. “ Ah !
me,” he will possibly observe—for fame doesn’t
always bring contentment, nor is wealth an
infallible cure for liver complaint —“Ah! me. 1
was happier when I played the bustle.”
* * * »
At Melbourne Princess’, also, there was a
considerable waking up on Saturday, when Elton
let himself loose in “ The Old Guard ” once again.
The celebrated ballet fetched the usual encore,
and those sweet girls showed no traces of fatigue
at the second time of asking. They soon kicked
over the traces, anyway. “ The Old Guard ”
seems none the worse for a withdrawal of Flora
Granpner in favour of Miss Marietta Nash (her
first appearance in Australia). The new lady has
a pretty, though weak, American voice, and
speaks very much to the point. Furthermore,
she won their affections right away with a
grotesque dance, and a certain genteel rortiness
that suits her particular style of beauty. Marietta
ought to do very nicely. Florence Young, Ida
Osborne, Howard Vernon, Ryley and Lissant are
back in their original places, likewise Bracy re-
appears in Leumane’s old part. The loss of
Leumane didn’t seem much of a calamity until
Bracy had settled down in his shoes, but if that
wandering prodigal were to return from his wattle
groves, with a repaired voice and a chastened
spirit, the public would very likely kill the fatted
calf in his honour. Meanwhile, Bracy’s polished
wooden appearance and washed-out falsetto have
!l £°od deal to do with prevailing depression.

* * * *
Music and the drama, and the nno arts,
th n Hme in Melbourne last week. Also
ae Circus made a gloomy reduction in prices, and
everal of the trained horses fell down for want
j* 1 Ppolic support. Every evening the city surged
‘P m a great, sweltering mob to the Exhibition
m 5 and sat gasping at a series of dazzling
pectaeles. A long, narrow platform had been
reeled under the dome. On the platform stood a
; ost w ith a hole in it, and through the hole a
r °P e was drawn, and attached to either end of the
Pe lay ten men, with the monotonous regu-
ofritA °f two strings of sausages. Each lot
f r ten was alleged to consist solely of patriots
,jJ? a Particular country, and the burning
, Sl l e °f every patriot was to weigh as
lcfl as possible and hang on to the rope as
hard as he could, for the honour of his native land.
Many nations were represented, and sometimes
the pair of rival powers immediately upon the
job would weigh so much alike and pull so ridicu-
lously similar that the rope simply stiffened itself
out and remained motionless as a corpse for an
hour right off. At these stonewalling times the
excitement of the vast congregation gradually
waned. A sort of cloud came over the entertain-
ment, and everybody seemed to lapse into mourn-
ing for the Duke of Clarence. But the nation
with the larger number of blisters on its fingers
would finally yield the requisite seven feet
of rope, and then a calico banner, dis-
playing the name of the victorious country
would be jerked along a clothes-line amidst
the jubilations of the populace. Occasion-
ally the tug-of-war was a simple matter
oh both sides. The Americans, or Irish, for
instance, would lie and hold the rope as though
they were a fishing-party, and directly they got a
bite they hauled in. The captured prey might
wriggle a bit, but it was poor, unevent'ul sport
for the anglers, anyway. As a feast for the intel-
lect of the looker-on this sort of encounter was,
however, superior to a stubborn contest, except for
those who had turned it into a gambling trans-
action, and backed the poor'fish. To thoroughly
enjoy a tug-of-war you must be an Irishman
or a Pole, or a native of any other country where
the heel of oppression has imprinted a strong
desire to get up and howl for your native soil.
Then you must make a point of seeing that your
compatriots can beat all the other peoples of the
earth at rope-pulling, and if you subsequently dis-
cover that the nationality of each team is hope-
lessly mixed and the glory of the conquerors
scattered all over the globe, so to speak—well, it
doesn’t much matter. The main idea is to feel
your bosom swell with pride at the time, and get
drunk for the sake of the dear old land.
