LYRICS OF THE STAGE.
(THE HEAVY VILLAIN’S SNEER.)
We’ve sampled villains by the score
Upon the modern stage,
We’ve seen them shedding human gore
In youth and middle age ;
Old, musty villains we have met
Whom mocking gods have “ guyed.”
We've never seen a monster yet
Who didn’t sneer (aside).
One villain had a raven wig,
Another’s hair was gray —
This villain was morose and big,
That small and full of play.
Their had deserts they always got,
They usu ally died,
But ne’er an one of all the lot
Forgot to sneer (aside).
Some villains sported choice attire
And patent-leather boots,
And some were objects to admire
In knickerbooker suits.
They plotted plots with subtle skill,
Suspicion they defied,
And seemed like decent people till —
Ha! ha ! —they sneered (aside).
’Twas thus they gave themselves away
And advertised their sin.
The villain of the modern play
Is very weak and thin.
To find a cause, from time to time
The hard has vainly tried,
Why men involved in secret crime
Delight to sneer (aside).
It is not done in real life—
The villain deep in guilt,
Who robs his pal and chokes his wife,
Is differently built;
Although his heart be cold as ice,
And inches thick his hide,
He scorns that old dramatic vice
He never sneers (aside).
Melb.
E.F.
At Poverty Point.
The Newcastle (N.S.W.) police lately made a big mistake
by arresting J. Summers, William Rignold’s business-
manager, on a false charge of wife-desertion. Mr.
Summers happens to be a bachelor—and the error arose
through mistaken identity.
One of the curiosities of Bathurst (N.S,W.) is the old
theatre in Durham-sfcreet, relic of the gold era. It has
stage, d.c., and pit still intact, though the walls are un-
safe. On its ancient board shone G. V. Brooke and all
the great lights of his day.
The catalogue given to visitors to the hulk Success
states that the bushranger, “Gipsy Smith,” was hanged
for the murder of his wife in April, ’6l. This is not
correct. Gipsy Smith died in the Melbourne hospital;
Smith was taken in hand after his discharge from prison
by Mr. Lang, a son of Dr. Lang, who entrusted him with
mobs of cattle from N.S. Wales to the Melbourne market.
The programme of the RAaliste Theatre, Paris, the
management and actors of which have just been sent to
gaol, was indeed a shocker. A correspondent has sent us
the programme, of which the barest hint cannot here be
conveyed even in French.
Compagnoni’s Hotel, Pitt-street, Sydney, bought as an
office-site by the City Bank Co., has an history. Thirty-
years ago it was the Shakespeare Tavern and was kept by
Tom Spencer, the clever mechanician of the Polytechnic.
In the days of the old Yic. the Shakespeare was presided
over by the well-known Polly Smith, who shared with
“Bob” Horne, a door or two lower down, the patronage of
the men about town.
One dark and stormy night recently a travelling com-
pany were billed to play some blood-and-thunder melo-
drama in a country town in New South Wales. When
the show should have commenced, the manager found
that the audience consisted of one solitary man in the
“ bob part.” The audience was offered his money back,
but he refused it and insisted on his rights, saying he
had ridden 30 miles and lamed his favourite mare, in
response to an invitation contained in the management’s
circular, and he had to see that show. The law was on
his side, and the company was compelled to act. All the
artists decided to do their very slummiestand worst, which
caused the audience to exercise his critical right, and he
hissed like 17 different kinds of ganders. Then a happy
thought struck the manager. He sent for the local police
force', and when he arrived had his audience ejected from
the theatre for disturbing the performance. Then the
manager and the leading lady went and liquidated the
receipts while props locked up the theatre.
Elton really goes to London this Easter. The little
man will show up the other little man.
Hans Phillips, sick these three months of rheumatic
fever, gets a matinee hen., at Melbourne Bijou, this
afternoon (Saturday)— “ Much Ado.” Hans has been 20
years on the hoards, hut never took a benefit. He
married a daughter of John Hennings. His father,
Alfred Phillips, was a well-known English entertainer,
and his mother was the true successor of Mrs. Glover, in
.’ 1, . Malaprop, and the like.
Madame Carandini has the ill-lucK to fall under the
up is tree of financial shade, wherefore Coppin, William-
son, and others, are taking steps to square her finances,
with a testimonial. More or less of misfortune has dogged
the whole family, and who does not recollect the brilliant
quartette of Madame, Rosina, Fannie, and Lizzie ?
indeed, there was a musical fifth , Tennie. Madame
Carandini was the original Fra Diavolo, in Melbourne.
Signor Carandini was a dancer, and appeared in the Mel-
bourne Royal Pantomime, 1855, as Harlequin.

Philip Newbury, a Maoriland singer of great promise, is
about to appear in Italian opera at the Shaftesbury
Theatre, London. Mr. Newbury is the son and heir of a
pastry-cook located in George-street, Dunedin. He is
small and fat, and suffers from an impression that he
owns the earth. The present writer met him first at a
secluded card-party, and when the gifted singer had lost
sundry shillings he got up on his ear and wanted to
wreck the premises, and then he chased the principal
winnerinto the street, and was only withheld from violence
by the most strenuous exertions on the part of the com-
pany.
W. G. Willis, the dramatist, the announcement of whose
death was lately cabled, was an uncommonly open-
hearted fellow. It is said that he used to keep his money
in his chambers in a tobacco-jar, and that his needy
friends were always welcome to help themselves as long
as the supply lasted. The only thing that ever astonished
him was the fact of any of his friends returning any such
loans.
The plucky Madame Simousen, after exploiting Europe
tor an opera company, failed to make it pay, and is teach-
ing music again. Her staging—very Punch-aud-Judyish
—did much damage, but it must be admitted that she
selected a most excellent team and dropped into dread-
fully depressed times.
There is quite an epidemic of “babbies” both coming
and come amongst the ladies of the profession in London.
We have already heard of Mrs. Langtry’s hopes in that
respect, and now a certain stately married woman has
had to take her name out of the Gaiety bill for the same
interesting reason. Better than all a certain dancer, very
popular in Australia, has had a baby. A popular manager
is the author of this work. Whose turn next?

of-war. They pulled Italy over, but one elephantine
Caledonian had strapped himself to the rope as an easy

was given the pull. Next night, Scotland sulked and
wouldn’t pull anyone.

Felix Tanner, who started on a 50 days’ fast at Sydney,

Gerald and Duff, who were accused of having eloped
from Broken Hill with the proceeds of a benefit which
they had given to the local Fire Brigade, have entered
libel-suit against the newspapers which published the
rumour. Their story is that they volunteered a benefit
to the brigade, the agreement being that they should
deduct the expenses, and hand over the balance. The
cash takings at the doors were £25 ; tickets representing
£23 were also taken at the doors, the latter amount being
for tickets sold by members of the brigade and which
were never in the possession of Gerald and Duff at all.
The £25 taken at the doors did not cover the actual
expenses of the night. The brigade, which took no risk,
benefited to the extent of £34, and never thanked Gerald
and Duff for it, hut assisted, so it is claimed, in slander-
ing them.
Ohas. Mathews, barrister, figures in a group of portraits
in the last Sporting and Dramatic News. Adopted son
of old Charley Mathews, real son of Davenport, Mrs.
Mathews’ first husband, American actor, but it is wonder-
ful what a likeness Barrister Mathews shows to old
Charley, who was very fond of him. He acted in Mel-
bourne, with his step-father, in ‘ A Bachelor of Arts,’
afterwards becoming Montagu Williams’ pet barristerial
pupil.

for “ the beautiful Evangeline,” they treated the reporters
to half-crown cigars, a phonograph and Burke’s whisky
(one of the brethren sometimes carried a flask in his
breast-pocket). “Evangeline” on the first night was
received with a howl of disappointment, yet the well-fed
local scribes gave it a column-and-a-half of unctuous
praise, describing it as “ the most magnificent comic
opera ever seen i;i Hid colonies.” One of the critics, wh.i
wrote a big notice, was asked on the quiet what he
thought of. the show, and he said sadly that it was
“ damned had.” Is it any wonder that managers secretly
despise the “art critic ” in Australia?

After the first six nights of “Robinson Crusoe” —
skilfully dished up by Essex-Evans— it was plain that
financial ruin was lurking round Brisbane Opera House.
In the middle of the performance one night in the second
week, when the ghost had refused to walk, two or three
principals in the leg-show would not come, up to the
mark, upon which they were soundly lectured by Jennie
Lee. The performance dragged its weary length along
until the-curtain finally fell over “The Birth of Love.”
The girls then mobbed Beverley, who made up his mind
to close the house, “ owing to the illness of Mr. Hickie.”
One lady (who cgn throw clothes-brushes with deadly
aim) threatened to “lake it out of Hickie,” but better
counsel prevailed, and some days afterwards she took
train for Sydney'.

At the Wallsend N.S.W. Colliery, when a
ballot was taken on the advisability of striking,
out of over 800 members only 406 rolled up to
record their votes. This tendency to shirk
exercising the duty of the franchise at critical
junctures is one of the most disheartening features
of democratic progress. What’s the use of fighting
for self-government if the democracy is too tired
to use the right when it gets it ?
The one really thrilling feature about
Stanley’s last big book is that he wrote it naked.
He took a house at Alexandria, and settled down
there to do his literary job in peace, far from the
madding crowd ; and he kept a nigger-boy to
scare off visitors. But one day a stranger per-
sauded the small African to take in his card, and
after he had waited a while in an ante-room the
great explorer came in smiling, clad in abso-
lutely nothing save a pen behind his ear. He
remarked cheerfully that it was a hot day, and
then just as he was going to sit down he suddenly
recollected the deficiencies of his costume, and
uttering an affrighted exclamation he fled out of
the door. A few minutes later he came back in
full attire and explained that he had grown so
accustomed in Central Africa to fly light in the
matter of clothes that he couldn’t get out of the
habit. Whether he'still retains the custom in
his hours of seclusion, and whether the Much-
Travelled Smythe does the same as a compliment
to his tame explorer, are among the unsettled
problems of history.
The great war between Chili and Peru is
already ancient history, but on the battlefield of
Tarapaoa, in the desert, the dead are still lying
just as they fell. There were four thousand of
them and nearly a thousand horses all left
unburied, for the Chilians were marching through
a horrible region of drought and death, and
had no time to dig sepulchres. But it never
rains on Tarapaca, and the sun has dried the
corpses and the nitrates in the soil have preserved
them, and up on the plateau five thousand
mummies lie in picturesque confusion with their
broken swords and bayonets, all as fresh-looking
as ever. There is no single bird or beast or
insect in that horrible desolation, and if nobody
interferes with the relics the Peruvians of the
century after next can call round and find their
dead great-grandfathers sitting up there as good
as new. But according to the stories that are
current in Valparaiso, the hair of the dead men
and the dead horses keeps on growing, till the
manes and moustaches and whiskers of that un
shaved army average some three feet or more in
length. Therefore, some day, an expedition will
probably go up and shear that deceased host, and
sell the defunct Peruvian’s beard to make bangs
and frizzes for the girls of Europe, and pay a big
dividend out of the enterprise. In fact, we an
getting out a prospectus.
Some of the worst enemies the Maoriland
colonists had to contend with during the war were
Englishmen, and perhaps the worst of all these'
was one Moffatt —from whom the Maoris learned
how to make gunpowder. Moffatt, who is said to
have been the nephew of Dr. Moffatt, the African
traveller, had at one time served in the British
army as an officer, and, according to report, was
estimated by English society as a most agreeable
and handsome young man; but nevertheless,
when he reached Wanganui he was soon set down
as a very undesirable colonist. He led an aban
doned life, and became so necessitous that he was
forced to make a home with the Maoris at a pah
up the Wanganui River. At this time the Upper
Wanganui tribes were hostile to the whites, and
Moffatt earned fame amongst them by teaching
them the arts of civilised warfare, which included
the making of gunpowder. Fame did not seem
to agree with Moffatt; he became an egotist, and
boasted that the most barbarous of Maori tribes
would receive him with open arms. This attack
of “ swelled head ” caused his death. He was
shown splendid specimens of gold, brought down
by Maoris from the Tnhua country. The Tuhuas
were pre-eminently hostile to the whites, and had
warned all pakehas to keep out of their country
on pain of death. “That means all whites hut
Moffatt, the Maoris’ friend,” said Moffatt, who,
setting out upon a prospecting expedition to the
T country, which lies in the vicinity of the
I ■ .hug mountains. Tongariro and Ruapehu, was
shot, and was relieved .of his heart, tongue and
eyes, delicacies for the Maori epicure.

Mr. Joseph Newman, one of the most
prominent teetotal advocates in Maoriland, died
the other day at Auckland from the effects of
drink. A big whisky-advertisement fell on his
head at a railway station, and he never recovered
from the effects.

an exception to this rule: one politician wanted
the title to please his sister,
Parkes’ gastronomical weakness is tomatoes
and sugar. When going a long railway journey
he, whenever tomatoes are procurable, gets a ease
of the finest ones possible, and at frequent inter-
vals cuts the fruit into slices, and piles each slice
up with as much sugar as will rest on it.
Sir Henry Parkes used to contribute from
Sydney “pomes” to the Launceston Examiner
(Tas.) nearly 50 years ago. One set of verses was
preceded by the remark : “In the absence of any
meritorious local productions we publish the fol-
lowing from the pen of a Sydney correspondent.”
It has just been elicited that when Super-
intendent Hare left Melbourne on the trip which
ended in the extermination of the Kelly Gang, it
was suggested to him at Spencer-street railway
station that a pilot engine should precede the
police train. “What’s a pilot-engine ?” queried
Hare.
Gustaf Dillberg and J. A. Philp, of Syd-
ney, inventors of a new printing- process, and
whose patent was opposed in England by the
powerful Linotype company, have come out on
top, the court having decided that the Dillberg-
Philp patent shall be allowed to stand, as a dis-
tinct novelty in the art of printing.
Allen, the author of the American Home-
stead Law, a measure which gave 100 acres of
land to anyone who guaranteed to settle on it,
died the other day in an Ohio infirmary, aged
81 years, while subscriptions were being raised all
over the country for him. He had been—he loved
the shears—a tailor and a newspaper editor.
John Kopp, of the famous Connecticut
(XJ.S.) Suicide Club, who had attended nearly aJ
the funerals of self-slaughtered members, has gone
to the tomb after their fashion. The club had
originally 20 members, but two only are now left;
all the rest have committed feo de sc. Poisoning
and drowning are against the rules as being too
easy.
When Agent-General Bray (8,A.) first
stood for East Adelaide twenty years ago, one
of the touts in an exciting contest paraded a
donkey through Adelaide with the inscription
‘‘ Vote for Bray.” Bray always maintained that
it was his singular name which Hist put him into
Parliament.
There -was an edifying spectacle in an
Adelaide restaurant the other day. A well-known
skinflint, a millionaire citizen, ordered two
penn’orth of cake on which he made his lunch. A
minute or two later you could see this millionaire
crawling under the table to recover the penny
change, which he had dropped on the floor !

Potts, who gave his name to Potts Point,
Sydney, was an early day accountant of the Bank
of N.S.W. He devised a system of interest cal-
culations, which was the vade-mecum of Sydney
accountants in the Thirties and Early Forties.
Darling Point, the habitat of the bloated Sydney
aristocrat, is an abbreviation of Mrs. —not Lady—
Darling’s Point.
Of the six men who have held office as
President of N.S.W. Legislative Council since
May, 1856 Alfred Stephen, John Hubert
Plunkett, William Westbrooke Burton, William
Charles Wentworth, Terence Aubrey Murray and
John Hay—the first holder of the office is the only
one still alive. The first four were lawyers ; the
last two died in office.
Graham Berry was once, for a short while,
worth <£30,000. Some of his friends allotted him
shares at opening prices in Ballarat and Reedy
Creek mines. His “Madam Berrys” cost him
Bs. and rose to .£23, when lie sold out. Then he
tried specs, on his own judgment, and the bottom
fell out of his luck. Berry, at 70 years of age, is
again a poor man.
The unintelligent comp, is as bad in his
corrections as in his blunders. Edward Ellis, of
Melbourne Herald, one day wrote some allusion
to “the Spanish succession.” The comp, soon
came down and said: “Look, Mister Ellis,
you’ve made a mistake. You write ‘ Charles the
Fifth’; that can’t be, as there was none after
Charles the Second.”
Sir J. D. Paul, the London banker, who
was awarded, with his partners, fourteen years for
fraud, was the author of “Ruth : a Poem,” “A
Harmony of Scripture,” and “Short Lessons for
Young Christians.” A Victorian bank-teller who
misappropriated .£2OOO used to keep a detailed
account of his peculations in a spare copy of a
Sunday-school class-register.
The cable which bores us with the news
of the death of every trumpery princelet and
every titled flunkey omitted to note the decease of
Charles Kingsley’s widow. She wrote “ Yeast ”
from her husband’s dictation, and copied “Alton
Locke ” for the press ; and the “ Chartist Parson ”
never hid his indebtedness to her largeness of
heart and brain. “Lucas Mallet,” one of the
rising novelists of the day, is a daughter of the
Kingsleys.
The death of Emile de Laveleye, the most
popularly-sympathetic representative of the new-
economic school, was allowed to pass with fifteen
lines of original comment by the daily papers of
the three principal cities of Australia. His work
on “ Luxury,” alone, if studied by every Austra-
lian workman, would revolutionise sociological
politics in Australasia. Do Laveleye’s separate
publications, which range from two-volume works
to eight-page pamphlets, and number seventy-five,
have appeared in the English, Russian, French,
German, Italian, Spanish and Swedish languages.
To the late Sir John Hay is due the credit
of having saved the maniac O’Farrell from the
hands of the Sydney mob at Clontarf. The lunatic
had been got on board the steamer when a wild
shriek of “ Lynch him !” was raised. The steamer
was a few feet from the wharf when the crowd
called upon the skipper to put back. He was
about to do so when Sir John—then plain Mister
—who was on tire bridge, peremptorily ordered
him “full speed ahead,” and he was obeyed. Sir
John was about the only prominent man who did
not lose his head that day.
Bir John Hay made his first big lun.p of
money out of a partnership with “Buck” Cock-
burn and Mat. Hervey, the well-known down
South pastoralists. Hay used to travel Port
Phillip country to buy bullocks and send them to
the Gippsland run of the firm, whence they used
to bo drafted in spans for teamsters. The
“ breaking-out of the gold” made a just paying-
concern into a gold-mine itself. Hay was the
only man of the triumvirate who stuck to his coin
for any length of time. Hervey died a mere
stock-inspector while touring a run formerly held
by himself—it was supposed from thirst.
According to an American publisher,
Rudyard Kipling, says London Figaro, “ wants
a dollar a word ’’ —that is to say A 2 per ordinary
newspaper line, or much over £ lOOO per Bulletin
page—“ for his stories.” Of course he does. He
wants as much as be can get. But no man in the
history of the world has ever got half the money.
Why, Charles Dickens wrote “ Hunted Down
a say, 20-column story—for a New York paper,
and got =£looo for it, which up to date is the
record price, so far as we have ever heard. Even
Tennyson did not get paid at the Kipling rate for
his charming little poem, “Crossing the Bar.”
The father of E. F. Hughes, the West
Victorian newspaper man, was a poet—a poet
among poets; for the bulk of his master-piece,
“The Millennium,” was not written at all, but
was literally “ composed at case”—each line being
“set-up” by the author in type as he arranged
the words. When “The Millennium” was com-
pleted, a complimentary copy was sent by the
author to that fine old pioneer, “ Frank” Henty,
and the poet eagerly awaited the ancient’s judg-
ment. At last the scribbler met the squatter.
“How do I like your poem, Mr. Hughes? Well
—h’m—ah—to tell you the truth, I am of opinion
that to be thoroughly appreciated, it will have to
be read in the millennium. One pain more or less
then won’t matter!”
Earl Jersey doesn’t seem to be a lucky
litigant. Way back in ’B9 he obtained a decree
for £633 costs against the Uxbridge Sanitary
Union, and ever since then his lawyers have been
vainly trying to get the amount from the U.S.U.
The fi.st effoit was to annex some special funds
belonging to the Union, and when this attempt
failed—with costs against his Excellency—the
lawyers laid legal hands on some land in the
possession of the Union. But the Court has
decided that Jersey’s costs are not to come out of
this asset, and the process has been set aside with
more costs against H.E. And now the judges
seem to think that it is not a good year for
Jersey’s costs, and doubt whether there is any
portion of the Union property that can be made
liable for the judgment.
J, G. Knight, the recently - dece t ased
Government Resident in the Northern Territory,
was a man of great resource. When he was in
business as an architect in Melbourne in the
fifties he erected the Criterion Hotel in Collins-
street, and Moss, the flaring Yankee who owned
the place, insisted on gilt balconies as part of the
adornments. The work was to be finished at a
certain time, and Knight found, almost at the last
moment, that the ornamental iron-work required
couldn’t be got for love or money anywhere nearer
than England. Moss nearly went into fits over
the delay, so Knight peacefully bought up all th«
iron bedsteads available, and cut the posts inn.
balcony-rails. It was a desperately expensive
device, but Moss’ hotel was ready at the contract

The publication of Burke’s Colonial
Gentry has revived once more the old question--
which is, after all, not a question at all—who was
Margaret Oatchpole?” One of her alleged
descendants—most assiduous in the repudiation of
descent from the celebrated and enterprising lady
—has informed “Burke” that his grandmother
was Mary , of a certain county ; but he
protests too much. When his grandmother wrote
to Bishop Broughton and asked him to correct a
statement which he had made identifying her
with Madame Margaret, he replied that he was
bound to accept her assertion that she was born in
another and more northern county of England
As a matter of fact, why should anybody object
to being regarded as a descendant of Margaret
Oatchpole? Lots of people who hold their heads
high to-day because of their alleged untainted
ancestry have no instance in the list of their
ancestors’ achievements of any act one-hundredth
part as noble as that which brought the heroine
under the ban of the law.
John Nicholson, S.M., of South Australia,
who stands a fair show of being appointed
Government Resident of the Northern Territory
was originally a Victorian State-school teacher!
He had the most erratic notions as to when
“ leave ’’ began and when it ended. Jack’s rela-
tions with the department were constantly
strained, and the officials were not at all sorry
when he gave himself wholly up to the fascina-
tions of a love with whom he had occasionally
toyed during his scholastic career—journalism.
By-and-bye he drifted into the editorship of the
Port Augusta Despatch, S.A., and thence
became a power in the wheat-land. The Wesleyan
interest has always had a sneaking regard for
Jack, though it cannot be said that he was ever a
particular ornament of the class-meeting. He was
one of the coterie of mutual admirationists which
—starting from Brown Hill, Ballarat—have done
more or less valuable work in several provinces.
The gang included James Campbell, ex-Post'
master-General, Victoria; W. M. K. Vale, for-
merly Victorian Commissioner of Customs; W.
H. Eitchett, parson and journalist, and soon to
be the Australian editor of that model of sanctified
pnggery, the Review op Reviews ; A. R. Eitchett,
now a Church of England parson in Maor-iland.
irom journalism, Jack Nicholson went on to the
magistracy, from which, if he does not jump to
the Residency of the N.T., he will vault into
something good.
What People Say.
“ Truth is stranger than Fiction,” so people say,
but such truth as we received from T. Channon,
Esq., photographer, Nowra, N.S.W., is only a
sample of what comes to hand by every mail from
all parts of Australia. Mr. Channon speaks for
himself : “I was one of the first attacked by La
Grippe at Nowra. The symptoms—sneezing,
running at nose, weak, watery eyes, frontal head-
ache, as if the brain wanted to press through, the
pain was almost constant, restless sleep, poor
appetite, constipation, foul tongue, constant ex-
pectoration of yellow, greenish phlegm, burning,
prickling feeling in the chest, great weakness and
pain at the nape of the neck, water —dark brown.
I tried to rise but was too weak, and had to lie
down again. Whilst at the worst, I read of the
curative properties of Clements Tonic, and was
induced to try it, although sceptical about
advertised remedies ; after a few doses I certainly
felt better, _so much that I wrote you, asking
advice, which you promptly afforded me. I
stated my case and you said it was a severe grip,
and recommended a continued course of Clements
Tonic. I followed your advice, and commenced
to mend rapidly and am now entirely recovered.
I only took four bottles, but am continuing it, so
as to thoroughly re-establish my strength (wise
man !) I can recommend it with the greatest
confidence for weak and delicate people.” Mrs.
Frances Rayner, Oxford Street, Paddington,
N.S.W., also writes, “I have suffered for many
years from dyspepsia and general debility, and
was induced to try Clements Tonic. I have
taken several bottles with such marked success,
that I have every confidence in recommending it
to my friends.” We must again warn the
suffering portion of humanity to be particularly
careful that the packages of Clements Tonic are
unbroken and intact. Several cases have come
under our notice where unscrupulous men have
interfered with the contents of the bottles, and
we now place a guarantee label over every
package, refuse all where this is broken. —F. M.
Clements, Melbourne and Sydney.

POLITICAL POINTS
see the pallid Prohibitionists, with Cheshire-cat
grins on their faces, rooting up all the vineyards.
The basis of all “ vested rights ” is law —
the breath of the legislators of the country. “ A
breath can unmake them as a breath has made.”
The operation of constitutional machinery is as
“sacred ” when it is negative and destructive as it
is when it is creative and positive. There is no
Tory argument that this bit of logic does not
riddle.
The Victorian Parliamentary Labour-
party at present consists of three members only
Hancock, Trenwith, and Dr. Maloney. The
Queensland Labour-party consists of about two
Glassey and Hoolan —but in both provinces there
will be big additions to the ranks at next election.
The Queensland Labour-party modestly calculates
on capturing nine additional seats.
H. H. Champion suggests a new kind, of
Parliamentary cloture for the Commons. Each
member’s share of the time there is minutes per
day ; he thinks therefore that it would be a fair
thing that any ordinary member who speaks for
say 40 minutes should be ‘shut up’ for a week
after so as to pull up his average. Every member
would require two slates then, one in the refresh-
ment-room, and the other at the Speaker’s desk.
President Harrison and Secretary Blaine
have, it is cabled, quarrelled over the Chilian
difficulty so seriously that Blaine now absents
himself from Cabinet meetings. The secret of
the quarrel between Blaine and Harrison is pos-
sibly the quarrel between their respective wives,
who have long been nagging at each other, and
whose latest casus belli is the hysterical support
given by Mrs. Blaine to Mrs. Maybrick’s petition,
which Mrs. Harrison violently opposes.

talked about amongst members of the British
House of Commons. So far, the chief proposal
seems to be that Lords and Commons shall sit
and debate in the same chamber, but vote
separately, as in the old Soots Parliament. Lord
Rosebery proposes to enable any peer, who wishes
to do so, to sign a deed surrendering for ever his

Into prohibitionists and local optionists, who hate

How long will it be before England follows
with respect to the Australian her own futile
experiment of 1778, when she sent a Commission
to the “rebellious” thirteen provinces of North
America offering to cede to them, totally and
radically, the legislative authority claimed by the
British Parliament ?
The “Yetus” letters, in the London
Times, exposing the rotten state of the British
army, are based on the report of the Parlia-
mentary Commission, headed by Hartington,
which report was squelched by the personal inter-
ference of the Queen, on behalf of her relatives,
Cambridge and Connaught.
The designation of Agent-General must be
changed for Minister, the office has so deve-
loped, since Victoria made the start, with Verdon,
who cadged the Nelson. Berry transformed him
self into a virtual Minister of the Crown, taking
important steps on his own responsibility, as in
regard to the New Hebrides.
The German Emperor's course will pro-
bably be exactly like that of Pins IX., who
began as an ardent Liberal, but became the most
violent of reactionaries, because, he said, the
masses were so ungrateful. Disraeli, at 70, wanted
to put the clock back 10 years, and he would
change the face of Europe. So with Bismarck.

electors may veto not only the sale but also the
manufacture of alcohol for beverage purposes.

The recent declaration of Andy Kelly,
M.L.A., that he was prepared to enter N.S.W.
Legislative Council in his shirt-sleeves, brings to
mind the historical assertion by the late 0. J.
Don, the Collingwood stone-mason, that when
elected to the Victorian Assembly he would take
the oath in his apron and working clothes; but
when Parliament met Don appeared in anew suit
of well-fitting black, black gloves, and a shiny
silk belltopper, much to the disgust of the
“ Platites.”
Labour-member Trenwith, of Vic., main-
tains that Protection makes commodities cheaper,
and yet gives the producer a higher price than
does Freetrado for what he produces. This is
done by wiping out the middlemen. The mer-
chants 'object to it, of course, because it takes
away their occupation, and brings producer and
consumer side by side. While Freetrade prevails
the merchant has a monopoly, because importing
requires a large capital, whereas every man can
start a small factory.
E. W. O’Sullivan boldly accused Sir Henry
Parkes in N.S.W. Assembly with wanting a pre-
cise knowledge of “everything outside British
political literature.” He need have made no
exception. Parkes has read with all the wide
desultoriness of a self-educated man, but also with
the self-edu. ated man’s absence of discrimination.
Consequently, his knowledge is confused, scrappy,
and ill-digested. People would be surprised if
they knew how large a proportion of his reading is
made up of works of the “Elegant Extract”
order.
When will the Australian democracies
learn that one of the essential principles of
democracy is simplicity of life? A fat salary
granted to any public official be ho Governor,
Judge, or that indispensable Judge’s associate, the
hangman—is a direct incentive to luxury, and
luxury is the child born of the liaison between
aristocracy and wealth. To attach “ allowances ”
to the emoluments of any post on the plea that
adequate representation of its duties demands
exceptional expenditure is to subsidise caste and
privilege.
H. H. Champion, in the Nineteenth
Century, says that the Labour Charter at next
general election will be—(l) Eight hours; (2)
Access to the land; (3) Abolition of the work-
house —pensions to be awarded in place of out-
door relief; (4) Graduated taxation of inherit-
ances and incomes ; and (5) Protective Legislation.
He thinks that a British Labour-Party in the
Commons could be made to go solid for the attain-
ment of its objects “with the most complete in-
difference to the convenience of Government or
Opposition.”
S.A. Audit Commissioners very briefly
show the difference between South Australian and
English finances :
The Imperial revenue is charged with the payment of
interest on the national debt, for which there are no re-
productive works worth mentioning, except the tele-
graphs, the profit on which is a very small item ; but the
debt here is also represented by railways, waterworks,
and other public works, which are worth more to the
colony than the total amount owing, and yield a net in-
come nearly sufficient to pay the whole of the interest.
This fairly states the case for Australia generally.
Rev. Cheong, Chinese preacher from Mel-
bourne, lectures throughout England against the
opium-trade. India gets nigh 10 millions sterling
a year out of opium-taxes, which it is considered
China pays, wherefore pious England forced
opium on China at the bayonet’s point and
cannon’s month. England went to war with
China, because Commissioner Lin, with Chinese
views of piety, destroyed two million sterling
value in opium, on English ships. But Chinky
Cheong must play light and show how not to do
it, because the Indian bishops are paid out of the
opium.
A meeting of Jackson’s Flat (Murrum-
bidgee) electors has decided to ask Arthur Rae,
M.P. (N.S.W.), to resign because he voted against
the Parliamentary vote of condolence to Queen
Victoria latelv passed. The Bulletin smiles.
The people who asked Rae to resign were not, you
may be sure, those who elected him. • Rae was not
the only member of Parliament who disbelieved
in the vote, but he was the only one who had the
grit to avow his opinions. Most of the rest stayed
away, and the balance weakly assented to a resolu -
tion against which, in a secret ballot, they would
have enthusiastically cast their votes,
Queen Victoria has been upon the British
throne 53 years, and during that period the nation
has paid lier (with compound interest) at the very
least 110 millions, while the sums paid to her off-
spring, pensioners, or other descendants, German
hr whatnot, must have run the amount up to
nearly double that figure. During the Queen’s
reign one billion pounds sterling has been directly
or indirectly filched from the British public in the
service of religion. Yet late calculations prove
that not one person in five in England ever goes
to a place of worship. In 1851, 37'3 per cent, of
the population attended a church of some sort;
1891 shows a decrease to 21 '3.
a O’Sullivan, during the fiscal debate in
N.S.W. Assembly, nailed Parkes’ monstrous lie
that the Protectionists of the United States were
the slave-holders, and that Protection was a
corollary of slave-ownership. J. C. Calhoun, the
very man singled out by Parkes for special men-
tion as a slave-holding Protectionist, speaking in
the Senate, in 1850, declared that “We (the
Southern States) must have free-trade, because
otherwise we derive no peculiar advantage from
the possession of ” —what do you think ?
“slaves!” The “ cheap nigger labour ” gave the
free-trader a supreme facility for getting into the
markets of the world. Freetrade—cheap 1 about)
everywhere.
Capital is further organising and console
dating in England. The British Shipowners’
Federation wants to ally itself with the
owners’ Association and the Manufacturers’
Union. With such a gigantic combination of
taskmasters, it is considered that the capitalists
would be able to pulverise the wage-earners and
defeat even a universal strike. So they might,
under ordinary conditions. But these two massed
foes confronting each other would present in
actual form the paradoxical problem of “an
irresistible force and an immovable body.” There
could be only one outcome to such a position, and
that —the physical outburst of the masses, a
revolution, in which organised capitaldom would
be broken up and swept away by a blood-red
blizzard. When both sides are fully organised the
whirlwind must come along.
The Australian daily papers {i.e., the
English papers locally established) gave, in a sub-
dued sort of way, their sweet approval to the
announcement that at Johnnesburg, South Africa,
an editor who had said some nasty things about
the recent Duke of Clarence had been mobbed
and ordered to quit the field. “ This incident,'
says the London press, “is an instance of the
universal loyalty of the British people ” (to mon-
archical institutions). Now, in this connection the
peculiar circumstances of the Transvaal must be
considered. The British there are under the
government of a race who whipped them at
Majuba Hill, and whom they hate; consequently,
the offence of the editor who threw bricks at
Clarence’s memory was construed by the local
Englanders (not Afrikanders-born, mind you, but
people imported as adults from England) as a
slap at their nation. And there you have it. In
Australia you can say exactly what you please
about the Duke of Clarence, or any other
potentate, and if one mob came to pull down your
office, another mob would roll up to “put a head’'
on them.

A 2500-head mob of cattle arrived at
Muswebbrook (N.S.W.) on 20th January from
M‘Carthy’s station near Burketown, a distance of
2300 miles. Sixteen drovers were in_ charge, the
journey took nine months and only 25 head were
lost.

7 We are requested to note that there was a
special service at Croajingolong Cathedral last
Sunday, in connection with the Duke of Clarence,

Professor Mobins, of Berlin, says :
“Tolstoi has unwarrantably generalised a few
facts of his own selection. To put tobacco, opium ,
hasheesh and whisky in the same category is a
complete mistake. Tobacco should be classified
rather with tea and coffee, of which, strange to
say, Tolstoi has nothing to say. These luxuries
bring about, according to the condition of those
using them, a slight excitement or a quieting of
the nerves. They do not stupefy ; their use in-
volves no condition of their abuse. W hat T olstoi
says about the moral disadvantages,of smoking is
mostly without proof of any kind. His premise
that tobacco used in the (Customary mannei
stupefies, is simply false.”
One of the oldest hotel identities in -Sydney
is Mr. W. W. Aitken, manager of Aarons
Exchange Hotel Company, and more familiarly
known to fame as “ Old Bill.” He has been con-
nected with the hotel for 20 years, serving first
under Mr. W. Cannon, now of Betty s; then
under Mr. L. Aarons, who, after some years of
unprecedented success, floated the concern into a
limited liability company, of which “Old Bill ” is
now manager. For at least two decad es the Exchange
Hotel has been in the front rank of city caravan-
serais, importing its own wines, spirits, &c.,
direct from Europe, and conducting its business
on a scale which defies competition. It is as
impossible to go there without meeting Bill as it
is to find Playfair without a cigar, or Jimmy
Mirams minus his umbrella. If one is to judge
by this company’s dividends, the management
can give all modern palaces a long start, and
overtake them without turning a hair.
THE CRAFT OF THE CRAFTY.
“Pa, what is the Agent-Generalship?
Does it sail the summer sea?
Does it e’er come south of the line for a trip?
Oh, tell me what it may be.”
“The Agent-Generalship, my son, is a shabbily-gilded boat
Whither Premiers flee when their day is done,
And their banks have blistered everyone—
To the Agent-Generalship they run
To keep themselves afloat.”

By T. P. O’CONNOR, M.P.
Price (post. jree),.ls. 2d.
“ Mr. O’Connor has certainly surpassed himself in the
writing of this intensely interesting work. ... No
newspaper notice, however full, can give any adequate
impression of the career as is here depicted of the great
Irishman who has gone. Mr. O’Connor’s book itself should
be in the hands of everyone who is interested in the
brilliant and stormy career which is ended for ever. . .
A delightful, graphic and biographical sketch of one of
the most remarkable figures of the century.”—Dublin
Freeman’s Journal.

Two absolute essentials for the existence
of the human race are—land and air. Many
economic and political systems have been invented
and are advocated for obtaining land for each
individual to live upon, but it has been left to the
Hydraulic Cooling and Ventilating Co. to intro-
duce pure air into every habitation in Australia,
by means of their “Perfection” Cooler and
Ventilator. By attaching this machine to your
water supply or tank, and simply turning on the
tap, a constant stream of pure, fresh air can be
obtained in any chamber you desire. Punkahs
are thus superseded, perishable goods can be kept
at a small expense without ice, and you can enjoy
the luxury of a cool chamber on the hottest day or
night. The machine, which is simplicity itself,
works at the cost of one half-penny per day, while
the price brings it within the reach of most.
Rend for particulars or inspect the machines work-
ing in our offices. T. A. Knight and Co., St.
James Chambers, King-street, Sydney.
Society
That jolly old missionary, Rev. Lorimer
Fison, relates how, on one of the South Sea
Islands, a wooden-legged man who had long and
vainly looked for a wife among the limited white
population at last concluded to marry a local nig.
who gave her consent. When the pair appeared
before the good pastor to be “spliced ” the man,
in reply to the usual question, said he “would,”
but the bride-elect, when asked if she would take
this man for her wedded husband, plumply said
“No!” “What do yon mean?” said the mis-
sionary. “All,” replied the recalcitrant bride,
“I have had a long talk with my people this
morning, and they told me I would be a fool to
marry a fellow with only one leg.” Hereupon the
bridegroom, seeing that the case was hopeless,
started to stump off—“ Pooh !” said he, contemp-
tuously to his lady-love, “If it’s only legs you
want, why the devil don’t you go and marry a
centipede ?”
* # *
A little Melbourne paper was amusing
itself a few weeks since by heaping scorn and
ridicule upon the cocked hat of a harmless consul
who represented in Victoria a republic consisting
of three men and a dog. Soon after this the poor,
vain consul died, whereupon remorse laid hold of
the little paper, which is a good deal like the
departed consul in its nature, and it rose upon a
pair of short hind legs to observe that the departed
object of its contempt was a good, kind man and
gave pleasure to all. Nil nisi honum is a healthy
rule if you upend it: Never say anything of a
dead man which would not be true and un-
nauseous if he were alive and kicking.

The expensive marble tablets bearing the
names of all the Sydney Mayors, and intended for
the local Centennial Hall, have been cast aside,
because the name of one ex-Mayor as inscribed on
his tablet is short of an initial !
* * •*
One hundred and twenty minute-guns
were fired in Sydney at the time of the funeral of
the Duke of Clarence. Take the cost of each dis-
charge, multiply it by at least 60 (GO guns were
fired at each British naval-station around the
world), multiply that by the number of naval-
stations, add a lot of other sixties for the exuber-
ance of British official colonial loyalty, and
explain what good this expenditure does either to
the living (who, as a London cable tells us, are
begging for charity owing to the want of employ-
ment caused by Court-monrning) or to the dead
Duke of Clarence, who, according to the Estab-
lished Church, is now either in Heaven or Hell?
But we forgot; every Established Church sends
every prince to Heaven.
# * #
The wedding-presents sent to the late
Duke of Clarence and his bride-elect are being
returned. The proposal to convert them into a
dowry for the penniless Princess shows how vul-
garly and brutally unsentimental a lot of people
are. To turn wedding-presents to so practical a
use would be like basing a wedding-breakfast upon
funeral baked meats, or making a wedding-ring of
gold filched by an undertaker from the teeth of
the dead.
# # *
The death of poor Prince Albert Victor
should prove a perfect god-send to Government
House, Tasmania, where, as the Viceregal Court
will have to go into mourning for some weeks,
the expenditure on sardine-sandwiches and rasp-
berries and milk for distinguished visitors will be
temporarily knocked on the head.
# # #
In the proclamation issued by the N.S.W.
Government announcing the Duke of Clarence’s
death he was referred to as the eldest son of the
Prince of Wales. At this rate “Wales” must
have at least two sons left. Who knows?
* * *
The Privy Council, as appears com the
full report of the case just received, quashed the
conviction in the N.S.W. (bigamy) case of Rev.
McLeod, with small ceremony. They didn’t
worry a fraction over the point that the local
Courts wrestled with, but just decided that the
section giving the N.S.W. Courts jurisdiction in
bigamy tvheresoever the second marriage took
place, was ultra vires if it meant what it said.
They thought, however, that it meant only
“ wheresoever within the local jurisdiction,” and
therefore was not u’tra vires, but was not nearly
wide enough to cover Dr. McLeod. It may be
remarked, however, that the text of the judgment
incidentally shows how deep and expansive is
Privy Council ignorance of Australian circum-
stances.

An elaborate swell who lives in Vicfcoria-
street, Darlinghurst, Sydney, returned ho ne after
spending a Ray evening. As he was fumbling
about to find the latch-key slot lie, with his left
hand, got hold of somebody’s beard. He instantly
concluded it to be that of a burglar, and a
desperate struggle ensued. The billy-goat came
out on top, as the swell was “a hit rocky on his
pins. ’ That goat is an evil and a desperate
character well-known to the police, and a warrant
will be issued for it as soon as the disabled potent-
ate gets well enough to swear an information.
The one Australian family, other than that
of Governor King, identified for three generations
with the Royal Navy, is that of Pasco. The ori-
ginal Pasco was Nelson’s original lieutenant at Tra-
falgar, and, gaining post rank, was captain of the
Hindostan, which brought out Governor Macquarie

at the Australasian Science Association at Hobart
last week, was witli Fitzroy, Stoke*, and Darwin
in the Beagle in the thirties and forties ; and his
son is navigating-lieutenant on the Palumah,
Queensland surveying vessel. Pasco (the original)
was, on half-pay, commander of the Victory at
Portsmouth, and during the later years of his life
used to have his dinner served while he sat in
Nelson’s chair.

Few people are aware, though many sus-
pected it at the time, that the late William
Macleay inspired that skit in Bell’s Like in
Sydney called the “Royal Ball.” It had refer-
ence to the ball which the ladies of Sydney
proposed to get up in honour of the Duke of
Edinburgh. It was he who invented all the
names of the ladies concerned, and he was par-
ticularly happy in some of them, such as Mrs.
Hideous Crimson, and Mrs. Saccharine Hitts.
Of course, everybody knows, or ought to know,
that Grosvenor Bnnster (or was it George
Morton ?) fired the bullets, but Macleay certainly
made them. Poor Tom Garrett was a kind of
editor of old Bell (now merged in the Sydney
Mail), and he was horsewhipped on account of
the article by one of Deas-Thomson’s sons,

which every historian of Australia since Went-

# # *
The Comores princes, exiles at present in
Noumea, went to a ball. A very pretty society
damsel was introduced to one of them, and in the
course of conversation asked him whether he was
married. “ O yes,” answered the Eastern prince;
“I have ninety-eight wives, would you like to be
the ninety-ninth ?” Tableau.
» * *
A genuine bit of tragedy was that which
happened in an across-sea province at “Cup”
time last year. One of the better stamp of
“ bookies ” was engaged to a young girl, and the
marriage was fixed for a fortnight after Cup-day,
providing he wasn’t “hit too heavy.” After the
ceremony, it was arranged they should go “home”
for a trip. Derby-day saw him a moderate loser,
but, to tease her, he exaggerated his losses in the
cable message to his sweetheart. She was sicken-
ing with influenza at the time, and the news so
affected her that she became alarmingly ill. W hen
his wire on the night of the Cup reached her
home, giving the aggregate of his winnings, and
stating that he had “booked a double berth ” on
an early Orient-liner for England, she was coma-
tose. Her lover landed in tune for her funeral.
Five years hence or thereabouts it will
dawn upon the lots of people who were interested
as depositors in certain Australian building and
investment societies that in becoming share-
holders in the reconstructed organisations they
played a beautiful game—for the original share-
holders. Before the reconstructions the depositors,
having first call on the assets, were in the posi-
tion of mortgagees in possession, and masters of
the situation. Now they rank with the mort-
gagors, and it will take another boom of tenfold
magnitude to the last to place them in as good a
position as they were before the panic. And the
era of booms has gone by in Australia.

Some time ago a Sydney man left his wife
a fortune, which, by one of the conditions of the
will, she forfeits should she marry again. A hook-
nosed, sharp-faced, full-bodied woman now sits in
the evening with four chappies—two on either side.
Those adjacent have each an arm around her neck
or waist; the outsiders each an «-rm around their
more fortunate companions.

“ The Honorable Thomas Reibey, of Eu-
tally, Hadspen, Tasmania, M.A. Trinity College,
Oxford, M.P. for Westbnry, and Speaker of the
House of Assembly, Tasmania, b. 24th September,
1821,” informs Burke that “ the original spelling
of the name was Raby, having been altered to its
present form by the Hon. Thomas Reibey’s father.”
Further he informs that ingenious per verier of
Australian ancestries, that “he, Thomas Raby,
married Mary Haydock, of Ipswich, Co. Suffolk.”
Now, we have before us Mrs. Mary Raby’s signa-
ture as attached to a document dated “Parra-
matta, Ist July, 1813.” Other signatures in the
same paper are Gov. Macquarie’s, Darcy Went-
worth’s, J. T. Campbell’s, Alexander Riley’s,
William Cox’s, Simeon Lord’s, Judge Bent’s, Sam
Terry’s, Isaac Nichols’—first Postmaster of Syd-
ney—all of which persons must certainly have
known Mrs. Reibey as Mrs. Reibey and not as
Mrs. Baby. Burke will please note.
A SUMMERY SONG.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
’Tis beastly hot,
Yes, very warm ;
I tell you what—
We want a storm
To brew and break
And cool the air ;
All night awake
I groan and swear.
When daylight steals
Across the sky
A fellow feels
Both damp and dry.
He’d fain expire,
And straightway go
To try the fire
That burns below.
Ah ! me, Ah ! me,
Twere truly nice
To simply be
A slab of ice.
Yet, by the bye,
I’d rather wish
To live and die
A frozen fish.
No appe-tite !
What can one eat ?
I loathe the sight
Of butcher’s meat,
And send away
The baker blue
With “Not to-day,
We're baking, too.”
My turtle dove
I can’t adore,
For making love
Is now a bore;
Though when last night
Her leave she took
I gave her quite
A melting look.
Excuse this rot,
I humbly plead:
The weather’s hot,
It is indeed:
Restrain your scorn,
And curb your wrath—
My muse has gone
To get a bath.
“NEC TECUM NEC SINE TE RIVERE POSSUM!"
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“ Quit my sight!” I exclaimed in my anger; “ begone !
Thou fury incarnate in womanly form !
I had rather through ages of loneness drift on
Than combat again thy fierce seasons of storm.
Thou hast made of my life such a hell upon earth
That no moments of bliss absolution can give ;
Take my gold, thou art welcome to all I am worth,
But go, for no longer with thee can I live !
“O return to me, darling !” regretful I cried,
“All the wrongs of the past are forgotten ; return
To thy place in my heart, in my arms, by my side ;
For thy presence, beloved, I unceasingly yearn.
And if passion betray thee to wrathful excess
1 will own I’m in fault and will freely forgive ;
Thou shalt conquer again with one tender caress—
Come back, for without thee no more can I live !
“ I banish thee now, but next moment deplore thee,
I fly thee to-day and to-morrow I seek ;
I am firm, but thy syren-like spell is cast o’er me,
Thou hast but to plead and 1 know myself weak.
So vanquished 1 yield to thy fell fascination,
I cannot escape, though in frenzy 1 try ;
It is madness to strive ’gainst embodied temptation—
I cannot live with thee — without thee, 1 die !”
A. V. G.
“The Kidnapped Squatter, and Other
Australian Tales,” by Andrew Robertson, is
admirably produced by Longmans, Green and
Co., London. The stories themselves are not
specially well-written, and are rather good material
for literary treatment than worthy specimens of
Australian literature. The tales are four in
number, and one, “Jack Reeveley,” is very good,
although a trifle improbable. “ A Bush Adven-
ture ” contains excellent raw matter for working
up by a practised litterateur.
“King Bungaree’s Pyalla ” and other
stories “illustrative of manners and customs that
prevailed among Australian aborigines,” by Mary
A. Fitzgerald-author of “Australian Furs and
Feathers is published by Edwards, Dunlop &
Co., Limited, Sydney, Brisbane, and London,
and is a capital collection of child’s stories, very
flimsily bound, and altogether inadequately illus-
trated. A book of this kind, if worth doing at
all, is worth doing well. The little volume
smacks of the soil, and the author is to be compli-
mented for opening up a hitherto unworked field
in Australian child literature.
In “A Poppy’s Tears”—Chapman and
Hall, London —that well-known story-teller, Man-
nington Caffyn, describes in exceeding good
English the horrors of opium-eating, or, what
amounts to the same thing, morphia-injecting.
The novel is interesting and highly dramatic, but
we are nevertheless at a loss to know what Mr.
Caffyn is driving at. He rails at his hero for
succumbing to what he calls a vice, but he proves
at the same time that his hero couldn’t help him-
self. Inherited tendency, &0., proved too strong
for him and he knuckled under. The good man
of the plot gets a lot of undeserved praise for
being what his parents made him, and altogether
the philosophy of the story is mixed—very.
Apart from the scientific aspect, the novel is well
worth reading, and shows that the author can
deal with the complicated interests of a long story
with considerable skill and much literary power.

* * *
The other day, in North Queensland, a
noliceman in the presence of a crowd examined
Fhftbodv of a “ found drowned ” and discovered
in the corpse’s belt a quarter of a terrace
house-that is to say, nearly 100 sovereigns. As
he eyed the assembled populace that energetic
officer sighed like a blast-furnace.
* # *
Melbourne Students’ Art Gallery is not a
nlace where you can spend more than a week
with much pleasure. Perhaps the students aie
somewhat hampered by want of models. The ones
they ha ve are principally four—a Mun ay cod, a lady,
a porter-bottle, and an old man. The cod they have
painted in a variety of attitudes, but always with
his head due nor’ nor’ east when the artist was to
the sou’ sou’ west. They painted him as long as
he kept anything like fresh. The man, too, has
undergone severe trials on canvas, and has had
to submit to a clean shave and other disguises.
To paint the lady’s portrait seems to be the first
effort of every student, and the result is exhibited,
all round the room. She is in various styles and
decrees of dress, and contrasting her differing
portraits a wild curiosity to know what in
Collins ■ street she is like leaves the critic
displeased and unsatisfied. Just as Melbournians
are as they wonder what on earth a syren really
resembles. The Waterhouse specimen has the
head of a private-bar maid adorned with the flying
hack-hair of a female-bounder, welded on to the
bodv of a mopoke. Ulysses must have been a
prize Johnnie if they had to tie him to the mast
for fear of his eloping with such a crowd as they
are represented. In fact, you can’t imagine any-
one desyren them. But the representation is pro-
bably as much like the common or garden syren of
Ulysses’ time as the steam syren whistle is like the
syren song. From the perturbation of U lysses
and his probably bald-headed crew we imagine
that those syrens were in very private-bars, and
perhaps some of them were not very much better
than they ought to have been, and would have
been if the Evangelical Grubb had been along a
few thousand years sooner.
* * *
It is a question which is the more awesome
spectacle, the Akgus in a humorous mood or the
Argus in tears. It wept, at one go, 16 awful
columns about the Duke of Clarence.
Sydney is infested with a class of men who
might be termed “ warehouse-buttoners,” only
they are better known as brokers and commission-
agents. These gentlemen haunt the leading hotels
on the look-out for country storekeepers, whom
they show round the “ trade,” get a good commis-
sion from the warehouse men for what the store-
keeper buys, and then very often assist the store-
keeper to knock down a big cheque. In Man-
chester they are known as “ hookers,” but in Man-
chester they often make several hundred pounds
a slap, which is never done in Sydney. Between
the buttoners there is no love lost. One of these
gentlemen picked up a storekeeper and proceeded
to show him round. Showing round means alter-
nately visiting a pub. and a warehouse. At a
private-bar the storekeeper met a buttoner who
had shown him round upon a previous occasion,
and called upon him to drink with them. “ Not
if I know it,’’ said the first buttoner. “You
won’t drink with that man in my company.
“ At least,” said the second, “ you will permit me
to speak a few words with Mr. Dash apart !
“Isit on business ? ” “Yes.” “ Then you can go
to Tartarus and wait until I have done with
him.”

People are speculating as to the names of
the four N.S.W. Inspectors of Agriculture just
appointed. It is rumoured that their appellations
are for the most part Maloney.
# # *
A writer in Sydney Sunday Times suggests
(in a long-primer leader) that the surplus sheep
and cattle of Australia be exported in the form of
sausages! No fear; Australia is not coming down
to compete with oats, dogs, horses, and Mysterious
Disappearances.

Now that the Darling rabbits have laid
waste everything possible in the way of grass and

The other night they surrounded a house and
while the squatter was asleep carried off his cork

The Argus has a hundred eyes — with a

* * *
Sir John Hay s estate should put a few
thousands probate-duty into the N.S.W. Treasury.
Sir John, in addition to stations, owned large
tracts of coal-lands in the IllaWarra district, and
his banking-account was one of the most solid in
Sydney.
JJ * * *
In point of a copiousness of expression
which is at once inane and vulgar, the corres-
pondence of “well-bred people’ read in late
London trials, could only be fairly compared with
the letters in the Mordauht and Yelverton cases
of a generation ago. England’s decadence is
be seen in the imbecility of her “ upper classes.”
# # *
A woman at Gullen (N.S.W.) had a little
girl born to her. This woman was a patron of
shilling-shockers, and the latest book she read
had a heroine called Narilga. The woman of
Gullen instantly wanted a Narilga in her family,
so she took the baby to a priest’s to have it
christened. When she was asked what the child
was to be named she said “Neuralgia,” and
Neuralgia she is, for though the holy father was
an expert in Latin, the divil a bit of Greek did he
know !
Ifc # *
“ Charlie” Barclay, managing director of
the Commercial Bank of Tasmania —who has lain
low for the occasion—has just fired off red-hot
shot in reply to the bombardment of abuse
levelled at him in connection with the closing of
the Y.D.L. Bank. He now makes it pretty clear
that the only enemies of the “ old bank ” were its
managers. Speaking at the half-yearly meeting
of his bank, held recently, he said : “Five days
before the (V.D.L.) Bank closed we lent them
U 20,000. .... I have now no hesitation in
saying that this amount was obtained by falsehood
and misrepresentation.” He further said that
the V.D.L. Bank “ was ruined by gross incom-
petency, reckless mismanagement, and negligence
which appears to me to be criminal.” Will this
well-aimed shot bring about a searching enquiry ?
* * *
The Commercial Bank of Tasmania', which
is now doing the biggest banking business in the
Island, has just declared a 20 percent, dividend.
The banking premises in Macquarie-street, Hobart,
which were believed to be commodious enough for
all time, are barely sufficient for present require-
ments, and the institution which began its career
in 1829 in a little shop in Elizabeth-street, under
the sole proprietorship of old John Dunn, whose
fortune was made at shopkeeping, will soon have
to find a larger building.
* * *
Apropos of the big hold which brothers
“Charlie” and “Dave” Barclay have upon the
Tasmanian Government through their bank, it is
interesting to note that when the bank was
started in 1829 the Government of the day refused
to recognise the paper currency which was issued
bv John Dunn, the founder.
J * # *
The V.D.L. Bank liquidators have de-
clared another dividend of one shilling in the
pound.
“ Ought the State to encourage marriage ?”
queries a faddist in the Sydney press, who wants
all Government billets filled by Benedicts simply
because they are Benedicts. Is the ghastly alter-
native indicated to be flourished in the face of that
tried and trusty bachelor, the unique and indis-
pensable Principal Under-Secretary Walker?

* * *
Twelve months ago the son of a wealthy
citizen of Sydney refused a gift from his father of
several thousand pounds, “to set him up in the
world.” He was the only son, and did not see
what was to prevent him getting all if he stayed
with the old man. To-day his father is married
to a girl of 26, and, after making a very good
settlement on his wife, has lost all his remaining
money. The son is eking out a small living as
agent for something or other, and the father has
to go down on his knees to Madame if he wants a
plug of tobacco.
In the Ghost number of the Review of
Reviews, it is related how a Mrs. Green, of
Newry, Ireland, received an apparitional intima-
tion of the death at Inglewood, Victoria, of a niece
and the latter’s companion. This is the only
“ghost yarn” in the collection that has the
remotest connection with the Australias. Few of
the “ appearances ” recorded by Stead are as well
authenticated as the experience of Archdeacon
Innes, of Hamilton, familiar to every resident in
Victoria. For a wonder Stead does not include
“Fisher’s ghost ” —which re-appears at least once
a year in the pages of some English periodical.

# # *
He lives respected in a fashionable Sydney
suburb now, the “ ringer ” of Queensland shanty-
keepers, and near his private graveyard stands a
thriving township. Goodintention was making
East and, contrary to custom, refused to drink, re-
markingatthetimethat beheld .£250. The poisoner
told his man to mark him, saying : “ If you bring
him back it is £5O for you.” The bloodhound—
an old-’un at the game—armed himself with a
bottle of prepared rum, cantered away through
the bush, met his victim, and they camped
together. After a pot of tea the rum was pro-
duced, and refused until Goodintention was
assured that it came straight from a storekeeper
in Blackall. He imbibed. Next day the shanty.
A week after —the grassy slopes of Paradise.
... At one time the shanty-keeper offered
the reserve price for a run then in the market,
but the vendor was superstitious and would not
accept blood-money.

MILLICENT.
(For The Bulletin.)
Laughing, flirting, beaming on,
Your new cavalier:
While he waits your whims upon—
Anxious, fond, sincere—
Has your memory no space
For the hours we spent
Sitting in that very place
Last month, Millicent?
I was then your devotee,
It was then your whim
To bestow those smiles on me
Now reserved for him :
With the favours you vouchsafe
He should be content;
For to him you give all you gave
To me, Millicent.
Just a tenancy at will
Of your changeful heart,
Glances bent with all the skill
Of the wanton’s art:
Just the right of flattery,
Of vow and compliment—
Right which you extended me
Last month, Millicent.
Soon he’ll learn that all your arts,
Are but trained to make
Harvest of devoted hearts
Now for triumph’s'sake:
Bitter thoughts will throng his mind
When he sees your bent,
He will think all womankind
Wanton, Millicent.
Vale! Without long regret,
Part we, Millicent!
I have learned your falsehood, yet
I am well content:
Scorned am I, yet scornful now
I proud lip can curl:
You’ve secured another beau
And I—another girl.
ISHMAEL.
“A DERRY ON A COVE.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
’Twas in the felon’s dock he stood, his eyes were black
and blue;
His voice with grief was broken, and his nose was broken,
too ;
He muttered, as that broken nose he wiped upon his
cap—
“ It’s orful when the p’leece has got a derry on a chap.
“ I am an honest workin’ cove, as any man can see,
It’s just because the p’leece has got a derry, sir, on me ;
Oh, yes, the legal gents can grin, I say it ain’t no joke—
It’s cruel when the p’leece has got a derry on a bloke.
“ Why don’t I go to work?” he said (he muttered, “ Why
don’t you ?)
Yer honer knows as well as me there ain’t no work to do.
And when I try to find a job I’m shadered by a trap—
It’s awful when the p’leeoe has got a derry on a chap.”
I sigh’d and shed a tearlet for that noble nature marred,
But ah ! the Bench was rough on him, and gave him six
months’ hard.
He only said, “ Beyond the grave you’ll cop it hot, by
Jove 1
There ain’t no angel p’leece to get a derry on a cove.”
H. LAWSON.
Sydney, January, 1892.

London Graphic praises Historian Lecky’s
poems and awards him a place “among our most
esteemed singers.” The world, on reading the
ineffable commonplaces put forward in justifica-
tion of this praise, will cordially damn the
Graphic as a literary critic.

shown a magnificent sample of cherries,” &c.
Shown? Wooh !
Spagnoletti: “ The Benvenue Captain,
going down with the pipe in his mouth, reminds
me of the coolest bushman I ever saw. He was
flung from his horse in Bourke-street, Melbourne,
but his pipe never left his jaws until he was in the
saddle again.”
Paterfamilias Cragg, head of the great
team of acrobats now in Australia, is 46
years old, and has been an acrobatic per-
former since the age of 15. He has been
married three times, and has earned quite a
fortune. The ears of the veteran acrobat who
does not wear a skull-cap for protection in
shoulder-somersaults, are often hacked and lumpy,
like those of a prize bull-pup. No two men turn
somersaults alike, and the acrobat who has
partnered many tumblers is likely to secure a
memento of each on his down-trodden head and
rasped ears. Cragg, senior, has no such scars,
and the reason is that he has trained all his chil-
dren from infancy ; consequently, filial piety—or
acrobatic perfection—restrains them from damag-
ing the bead of the family. Father Cragg has
been a good boxer in his day (which from his
healthy appearance might be now), and for some-
time he held the amateur championship of Man-
chester, England. He tells an interesting story
of the Dempsey-Fitzsirnmons’ fight. Cragg was
performing at a theatre in New Orleans when the
news of Fitzsimmons’ victory came per telegram.
He was asked by the manager to break the
news to the audience. The audience was simply
struck dumb, and to fistic America, which had
backed him to a man, the snufting-out of the
Nonpareil was a phenomenon as bewildering as
the eclipse of the sun was to the ancients. Cragg
holds a high opinion of Mr. Paddy Slavic. Black
Jackson, he affirms, breaks out on such howling
jamborees at intervals, that his days as a fighter
are numbex-ed. “The Graggs in dress-clothes”
first appeared on the play-bills some nine years
ago, when the family took the lead at an Ameri-
can theatre. All rival acrobats immediately
followed suit, with a view of “trading on their
cloth,” but failed to score points by doing so.
None of the professionals seem to i rain on
orthodox lines. The Graggs always drink and
smoke just as much as the ordinary athlete does
out of training, but they are particular in taking
all precautions against catching cold. Cragg says
he has never seen a man killed or badly injured in
acrobatic feats. The first accident to his family
happened lately at the Royal, Sydney, when his
eldest son strained a foot-tendon ; this, however,
does not debar him from going through his usual
programme. None of the papers have mentioned
that one of the Graggs is a daughter—Victoria—
born in Sandhurst, Vic., 13 years ago. She is a
wonderful acrobat —especially for a girl. Her
father says that it is impossible to train a girl to
the pitch of perfection obtainable in a boy.
Although the audience may not detect it, yet a
girl tumbler is clumsier, less springy, and far
heavier than a boy. A girl doesn’t tumble to it
like a boy, you see. The fact is that the parent
heart of Father Cragg is locked up in “ dat boy,”
Arthur. The ten-year-old prodigy is barely four
feet high, and 351b. in weight. He has been
schooled in tricks since the age of seven months.
He was 18 months old when he made his first
public appearance, and the audience laughed so
that he was seized with stage fright, ran off, and the
largest lolly obtainable wouldn’t beguile him into
facing the music again. Arthur’s vice is cutlets
or grilled chops. He likes just three for break-
fast. Soon after the war the Graggs visited
Egypt, but only to sink 9000 francs in four weeks,
and to land in Dunkirk, France, with hardly a
coin in their pocket on Christmas day. One
special feature of the Graggs’entertainment is that
when lyjng on their backs doing foot-work they
use no shoulder nor loin supports. The Graggs
have been unto the uttermost ends of the earth
which yield pay-dirt. When Cragg, sen., visits
the North Pole, and poises himself with his back
upon it, his boy prodigy will steady himself upon
his father’s Heaven-seeking sole, start forth like
a boomerang towards the Polar Star, and come
back doing “ flip-flaps ” over the Aurora Borealis.
When the eyes become weak or the lids in-
flamed and sore, a scrofulous condition of the
blood is indicated. For scrofula, in all its forms,
Ayer’s Sarsaparilla is the best remedy. It vitalizes
the blood and expels all humors.

Little wonder that the world at large is apt to
mok upon the average Australian sporting writer as only
a shade above a jockey or trainer. The gross illiteracy of
manv of these gentry is pitiable, and is a standing testi-
monial to the ignorance of the people who own the
snorting papers (Australasian excepted). Hardly one of
them for instance, seems to know the difference between
‘‘lav'” and “lie.” “The course lays near So-and-So
“the race lays between Take-’em-down and Hardpull,”
are sentences you read every day in one or other of the
papers. # # #
Jt was at a suburban pony meeting recently
that the “push” had a real good thing and backed it
heavily but it did not come off, as their pony was beaten
bv a short head. After the race the following conversa-
tion took place between trainer and jockey. T.: “ Never
saw you ride a worse race.” J.How’s that? I couldn’t
keep him further hack without pulling his head off and
running the risk of us all being sent up.” T. : “ What
are you talking about?” (and then in a tone of horror.)
« you don’t mean to say you didn’t try.” J.: “ Try be
Plowed, didn’t you tell me you would give me the office
as I went to the post, and I’ll take my oath I saw you
wink as I went through the gate.” The trainer’s further
remarks are unAt for publication,

For ways that are dark and for tricks that are
oronk commend us to pony-racing. Recently a galloway
won in the 14.1 class. When a wager was laid that the
pra( j » was at least 14.3 the owner wanted a month to
get ready. And what does “getting ready” mean?
Why touching the moke up after the most approved
methods with a hot iron—and so on. Every winner
should be compelled to again pass under the standard
after scaling. *
# # *
The San Francisco Examiner reports that
Johnel Sullivan has “come back from Australia with
wrath and side whiskers.” Then it interviewed him:—
“The Australians,” said Sullivan, “are the and
John launched forth into a string of condemnatory vitu-
perativeness that would have knocked any less remote
island off the face of creation as he spoke. “You may
print all that and say I said it,” he concluded, “ for of
all the loafers on this earth the Australians take the bun.”
Nothing could be plainer, nothing more palpable, than
the fact that the world’s greatest slogger felt overcrowded
and out of place on this earth as long as Australia existed.
His great hand sank with a Aop on the marble desk as he
accentuated his remarks with a thump that made the
building quiver. At the mention of the antipodean con-
tinent one could almost see the nap of the champion’s
silk hat stand up and bristle with indignation. “They
set about boycotting me before I reached there,” he ex-
plained. “The papers began it, and the people took it
up.” Here follows a violent diatribe on the way the show
was managed. John L. was so patently glad to be back
in America again—to be anywhere on this green earth,
■or out of it, away from Australia, that it was
obviously superAuous to ask him how he liked
ooming back. Nevertheless he spontaneously volunteered
the information that he was glad, profanely glad, to be
back in the States again, and feel beneath his solid feet
the free soil of America. He pensively twirled his cigar
in his mouth as he spoke, and again consigned Australia
to eternal torment, and characterised its inhabitants as a
“ set of bums.” In fact, every invective known to
the American and Bostonian vocabularies John L. Sullivan
hurled against the absent continent, its population, its
theatrical methods, and everything pertaining thereto.
He had no use for any of them, and made no secret of
that circumstance. In his own words, the mere thought
of the place made him tired. He was cold sober when he
spoke. He had taken but one drink, in fact, since he left
the antipodes, and that was on his own birthday, which
was an occasion celebrated with great solemnity on the
steamship Alameda.
# * *
TheN.S.W. Cavalry tournament, at Rosehill,
last week was a tremendous affair. All sorts of events
were going on at once all over the place. While some
excited parties were wrestling on horseback in front of
the grandstand, a jumping competition would be in full
swing behind them, and away to the right a line of lancers
would be tent-pegging ; in the middle distance a sweating
mob of artillerymen would be dismounting their guns
against time, and on the distant horizon Aights of blue-
jackets mounted on bare-backed troop-horses could be
seen charging wildly in every direction, hogging their
horses and hauling frantically at their “ rudderlines "
without the slightest effect. And yet even with all these
diversions people got very weary of the show. One tent-
Ser is much like another; lemon-cutting and Turks-
slashing pall upon the jaded taste; and the only
really “fetching” events in the affair were the blue-
jackets’ race and the wrestling on horseback. The blue-
jackets numbered 80, and from their style of riding it is
about a fair estimate to suppose that 25 of them had never
seen a horse before. They were hoisted up on their
steeds, got a good grip of the mane with one hand and
instantly began to flog with the other. They were dog-
ging before they started, and the horses were rushing
about wildly, each thinking (and with very good
grounds, too) that the devil was on his back.
How they ever got into line for a start was a
miracle, but the starter had the bugle signal for “halt”
Bounded, and the troop horses immediately propped dead,
in most cases shooting their riders out on to their ears.
As soon as they had scrambled back, the order “ charge ”
was given, and every horse set off as hard as he could
hok. It was God’s mercy that none of the bluejackets in
the front of the race fell off, as, with about twenty-Ave
horses behind them, they would have been trampled to
mummies. Only one horse ffnished riderless, but some of
the old nags, as soon as they passed the post, slewed sharp
off for the gate, and shot their riders yards in the air. A
lot more went round the course again and rode a second
nmsh. The leader, who was hanging on by teeth and
nails, raised his head and cheered as they passed the
stand. His horse immediately charged in among a troop of
lancers who were performing evolutions, and we saw the
chest of his pants flash in the sunlight as he went down
nows first right among the astonished lancers. No one
was hurt. Gov. Jersey was there on Saturday. On
rnday, one of his aides, Mr. Gosohen, won the polo
stiok-and-ball race in good style, on a very nice-looking
Pony, but on Saturday, when all the society-folk were
present, the “ aides’ ” ponies were donkey-licked by a
'jP?looking, grey animal, bearing the appropriate name
™ The Shifter. The blue-jackets, artillerymen, and
Ir valr y me n fairly made Parramatta howl, during the
fhl e f d ? yBth ?y were there. A Murrumbidgee man won
ne tent-pegging, a Victorian the Turks’-head slicing, a
arramatta man the lemon-cutting, and a Camden horse
on the jumps, so things were pretty evenly divided.
