# # •
A queer Sydney institution is the Model Yacht
Club—a number of people (not little boys, but great big
grown-up men, some of ’em with two-feet beards) —who
sail, on a puddle at Moore Park, little boats that you could
put in your waistcoat pocket. One of these potentates
has been to England, taking with him (in his cigarette-
case, probably) his “fast yacht Flying Cloud,"’and has
brought it back after winning 14 first prizes out of 16
races. “ Hooray for champion Australia 1” again shrieks
the press.
* * *
Dear Bulletin, —The prize for meanness has
been earned by the Sydney Association Cricket Ground
authorities. A number of trees utilised by the impe-
cunious to enable them to view the various amateur
athletic events on this ground, have been chopped down.
They were situated outside the dividing fence, between a
Bishop Smith and nothing, and represented possibly
£1 when the d.c. and gallery of each was filled. By the
way, why is not a gate placed on the eastern side for the
convenience of the eastern suburbs? —Sans Souci.
* * *
So the secret is out why Slavin’s backers have
“ turned dog ” on him. After his arrival in America he
was pitted against Jemmy Carroll, of Brooklyn. The
latter did as he pleased with the Australian, and but for
the intervention of Charley Mitchell would have knocked
him out. Of course, Slavin may have been in bad con-
dition.
* * *
When the cable announcing that Mitchell had
given Slavin a hiding on the Liverpool stage reached
people anticipated that the Australian would give “ rats”
to the Englishman—if a Brummagem Irishman can be so
called. But he hasn’t, as yet, and they say that Mitchell
and Pony Moore, his father-in-law, are now getting the
cream of the Slavin boom, and that when the latter is
played out Chawley will gain further glory by putting a
bigger head on the already bigheaded Australian.
# * #
Dear Bulletin, —Your correspondent opines
that Jackson will defeat Slavin if they meet, and that the
latter’s rushes will not come off, as he is too slow’. The
idea of saying that Slavin is slow ! I was present when
he fought Goode and big Joe M'Aulifle in England. Why,
the man hits like a flash, and is more like a demon in the
ring than anything I know. He is just the style of man
to worry Jackson down, taking a line from the latter’s
goes with Joe Goddard and Patsy Cardiff; and good
judges do not think Slavin w’ould have much trouble with
either. It must be remembered that the Slavin of to-day
is a different man from the Slavin they used to see at
Foley’s some three years ago. Then he was young and
raw and had not the full use of his strength. Since then
he has improved wonderfully in quickness, science, and
confidence, and I prophesy that when they meet it will
be a had time for Jackson. —Gumleaf.
# # #
One reason assigned for the unwillingness of
Mr. Piesse to back Slavin in America, is the fact that the
former presides over an English sporting club, and
naturally would wish to bring grist to his own mill. But
the excuse won’t stand. When Kilrain, an inferior man
to Jackson and Slavin, was in question, Slavin found
backing enough to fight in America.

In a Sydney second-hand shop the other day
was to be seen the cup, won in 1888 by the Assegai, the
yacht belonging to W. P. Smairl, of building society fame.
A ticket notified that the trophy (valued at £200) could
be had for £35.

Why is Larry Foley on the retired list as a
pugilist, yet willing to take on all-comers at foils, single-
sticks, &c. ?
• * *
Paddy Doyle, the aboriginal who, in the
opinion of many, won the last Botany Gold Cup, which
was awarded to Frank Arnold, is said to have recently
died in the Mudgee Hospital. The poor blackfellow gets
nothing but dissipation out of foot-running.
# * #
Gormley’s swimming feats at Sydney last week
are world-records: 200 yards, 2min. 22sec. ; 220 yards,
2min. 38Jsec. Oavill easily defeated Corbett in Grain. 53sec.
over 500 yards.
* * *
“Ernie” Hutton, who made a big score for
Melbourne in the inter-University match, the other day,
is a coming interprovinoial and international man. He
never fails to make runs, has some century records, and
is, in the presence of a crowd, as cool as the grand-stand
lunch.
* # #
Stoddart and Lohmann are the two best-looking
men in the English eleven. In the big match in Mel-
bourne, Stoddart fielded close to the reserve where the
local nymph could admire him with the nude optic.
# # •
South Australians certainly give George Giffen
credit for a wonderful knowledge of cricket. They say
now that he didn’t exert himself to make runs in the
international because he foresaw that they wouldn’t be
wanted. Where was he the night before ?

In 25 years the burly and surly W. G. Grace
has made 86,221 runs in 846 innings.
* * *
Foot-racing, which has lately been at a low ebb
around Sydney, shows signs of a revival. The Botany
people are going to run off a £l5O handicap in March.
* * *
Considering the dodges—some of them exceed-
ingly cruel—now resorted to to bring ponies below a
certain height, these animals should be classified by
weight. The task could be accomplished with very little
trouble.
# * *
The principal item on at Foley’s on Saturday
night was a finish-fight between “Yankee” and Thomas,
a pupil of Frank Pablo’s. They met a few weeks ago,
when Thomas, though winning, had to give in through
sheer exhaustion. On this occasion he did not run the
risk of getting worn-out, as he forced the pace and
knocked out his bigger and older opponent in four
rounds. With careful tuition, the winner should be
heard of again.
• # *
Bob Fitzsimmons is to fight Maher, the Irish
champion, for £2OOO.
* « •
Grace went all to pieces as a bat during the dog-
days of last week. “A child could slay Richelieu now ! ”
—a junior kiddy, first ball.
* * *
Slavin was recently run in, with some “ swells,”
for being drunk. Another Australian champion recently
got safe lodgings in a Sydney police-station.
* # •
Plunger Brodribb, even though he did well in
silver, has not been a lucky man. After selling his white
elephant, Titan, at a big loss, he must now feel sore to see
the animal very much fancied for next Newmarket.
# * *
Tom Lees is meeting with much success in his
attempt to open up a boxing and athletic business in
W. A.
# # #
Mick Dooley seems to suffer from what actors
call footlight funk. In the open he always has shown
wonderful gameness. On the boxing stage, surrounded
by a crowd! his nervousness overpowers him. Otherwise,
where would Slavin, Goddard and Jackson be now'?
* # #
Phillips, M.C.C. professional, refused to play
for Victoria in the Sydney match, his fee of £lO 10s.
having been cut down to £6 ss. by Victorian Cricketers’
Association.
* * •
Old Tom Reibey of Entally (Tas.) who once
owned Stockwell and Malua (but he didn’t breed
them) still does a little racing, but the “all rose” seldom
shows to the front. The old gentleman is much too funky
for a racing man, and probably, as a rule, nominates just
for the sake of seeing his name on the Ust—nine out of
ten of his nominations are scratched. Ha is acknowledged
to he a first-class judge of a youngster, but. i« too
parsimonious to keep aristocratic stock, and pins his
faith on showy animals that never score a win.
•' # #
Peter Maher, the Irish champion, lias challenged
the world to box or fight in any style. Maher is only a
middleweight, after the style of Mace and Sayers, and
every ounce of weight tells in a Queensberry contest. A
few years ago he was defeated by Hair for the English
amateur championship, and Jackson, on an exhibition
tour, knocked him out in Dublin. But he has since im-
proved a good deal. In London he made short work of
Gus Lambert who gave Jackson some trouble in America.
Since his arrival in America Maher has easily beaten half-
a-dozen heavy-weights. He is being engineered by the
astute Billy Madden, who may be said to have brought
out Johnel and Charley Mitchell. This fact alone shows
that Maher must be a good man. v
• # »
George Giffen, according to his fellow players,
was so incensed at the way the Sydney “larrikins”
barracked him that he made up his mind not to play
against England in Sydney at any price. George is
usually stubborn, but in this case wiser counsels pre-
vailed.
* # #
On the night of the Cavill-Corbett swimming
match, in Sydney, L. Parkinson twice dived from a height
of some 48 feet into 8 feet of water. Diving from great
heights had a big rage in America some years since, and
pretty well all the cranks who took to the game came to
grief sooner or later. Verb sap.
* * *
The Melbourne tug-of-war is wholly inconclusive.
The French were Arabs and Algerians—some from Sells’
Circus—while the Germans worked on a system of prac-
tised knack, which gave them a favourable handicap.
The A.N. A. offers a £2O prize tor any team to beat the
victors, and confidently expects natives to do it. This
kind of thing needs to be rehearsed.
# # *
The tug -of - war is such a monstrously
big thing that other entrepreneurs wonder it never
occurred to them. The Bulletin predicts the establish-
ment of an inter-provincial tug annually. The Melbourne
promoters commence business in Sydney at the Exhibition
Building on 6th Feb., with a £l5O prize to the winning
team.

Jack Hall, perhaps the cleverest lightweight
yet seen in Australia, who, for the last few years, has
followed up bookmaking, is anxious to re-enter the fistic
arena but cannot find an opponent.
The A.J.S. Bank officers are wondering
why their bonus on last year’s salary is only five
per cent, instead of 10 per cent., as usual. Does
the reduction, they ask, foreshadow the utter
abolition of bonuses, or are they now being made
to suffer for the rascality of ex-officer Cole, who,
despite that “infallible system of check,”
blasphemously annexed .£BOOO of the corporation’s
good red gold, right under the nose of Manager
Dibbs?
One of the many vagaries of a well-known
Bombay paper was perpetrated twenty-odd years
ago. The editor quarrelled with his partner, the
manager, and each appropriated a column to dis-
cussing the character of his partner, and to taking
the public into his confidence as to the causes of
the difference. The thing went on for some
weeks, until at last, the humour of the situation
having evaporated, fifty subscribers made an
effectual appeal to the two dissentients by sim-
ultaneously withdrawing their names. This
reminds us that a present-day Sydney journalist
once, in his callow journalistic day, ran on salary
a newspaper in a Victorian township. Under the
terms of his agreement, he had complete control
over the editorial “and news columns,” but on
one occasion he gave great umbrage to the pro-
prietor by taking up an attitude on a municipal
question opposed to that assumed by the latter in
the local shire council. The proprietor traversed
his editor’s view in a letter, which the scribbler
in a par., acknowledged having received, but pro-
nounced “to be unsuitable for publication because
of the unnecessary warmth of language used by
the correspondent.” Not to be outdone, the pro-
prietdr inserted his own letter in his advertising
columns, and appended to it a copy of a note
which he had addressed to the editor that day.
Needless to say what the note contained— a
mouth’s notice.
The Bulletin now sees clearly what is
coming out of the Lindsay “ exploring ” expedi
tion. A reef, capital .£500,000, in £1 shares.
The most beautiful edition of Tennyson’s
poems yet published is a pocket volume, morocco-
bound, rounded edges. It was specially issued so
as to include that lustrous gem, “ Crossing the
Bar,” in a complete edition.
The officers of the British army are said
to be divided off into various syndicates and
limited companies, each interested in palming off
its own particular brand of inferior cannon, cronk
rifle, or putty “short-arm” upon the wretched
War Office.
By a decision last month in a London
court, the law is made clear that when a news-
paper publishes a report of a counsel’s address,
the allegations in which are not subsequently
substantiated by evidence in the case, “privilege”
does not govern the publication. The pasting-up
of this item in the offices of those Australian even-
ing newspapers which are sub-edited by “ boy-
labour,” should save a few thousands to the re-
spective proprietors.
The hopeless snobbery and irretrievable
hypocrisy of the present British social system
never received a happier illustration than the
circumstance that the name of the girl alleged to
have been seduced by Earl Russell was published
in the divorce-court and in the newspaper reports,
while that of the aristocratic libertine whose house
at Ascot (presided over by his mistress) was
visited by Lady Russell and her mother, was not
mentioned in examination, or by any daily paper.
The hectoring military cub who ranks as
first-born to Emperor William is the subject of
some infinitely touching anecdotes in a London
paper. One delectable little yarn tells how the
infant clapped his hands together when somebody
described Heaven as the “Golden City,” and
yelled, “How glad I shall be to get there ! For,
won’t I throw heaps of gold down to the poor
subjects here!” The sucking autocrat has already
swallowed the notion that he was ordained by
God to chuck other people’s money about.
The astonishing enlivenment of the pages
of the Times under its new management is
peculiarly-well illustrated in its law reports,
which, always the most admirable of their class,
have been remodelled till they read with a
“swing’’and “go” little short of dramatic. In
the recent slander-case, Mrs. Osborne against the
Hargreaves, who accused the plaintiff of stealing
Mrs. Hargreaves’ diamonds, all the by-play of
voice and gesture which so illuminates the dreari-
ness of court-procedure, and which often decides
the issues of a trial, is so well transcribed that the
lengthy report holds the attention like a shilling
novel.
The Calcutta Statesman, one of the best
of the Anglo-Indian newspapers, is owned by the
Maharajah of something, a wealthy minor poten-
tate. Twenty years ago, it was edited by Mr.
Robert Knight, a man who ran the gamut from
the church to journalism. He was a strong
advocate of pro-native views, and was the one
Englishman in India who held that had the
“ mutiny ” been the breaking out of a native force
against conquerors of any other European race
England would have applauded the revolt. He
was strong enough to express his opinion, but not
strong enough to hold his opinion in face of the
social ostracism which was extended to him. He
recanted, but only the other day his leader
translated into the vernacular was reprinted with
one sentence of comment by a Bengali editor.
The comment freely translated was: “We will
keep Mr. Knight’s words before our masters.”
A purgative medicine should possess tonic and
curative, as well as cathartic, properties. This
combination of ingredients may be found in Ayer’s
Pills. They strengthen and stimulate the bowels,
causing natural action.
A Narrow Squeak.
Dear Bulletin,— Re “ Malchook’s Doom,” your
story of the Nicholson River (N.Q.), I will tell
you a true adventure of an old friend of mine,
who camped on that river during the wet season
of ’B2-’B3 with a mob of store-cattle for the
Northern Territory. The blacks had been very
troublesome and had paid one or two visits to the
camp, as well as spearing many cattle. G. used
to sleep under a tarpaulin stretched over a ridge-
pole, the ends being open, and the sides also, for
about two feet from the ground. All the rations
were stored there, and G. had a bunk rigged up
on the spot. One night, after getting inside his
mosquito-net, he remembered that he had not put
his revolver under the pillow. He felt, at first,
too lazy to get up and get it, but finally, under
the pressure of a presentiment, he did so, and fell
asleep.
Now, mosquito-nets in the Far North are
generally made of coarse cheese-cloth to keep the
venomous little sand-flies out; the top and bottom
are of calico, with a slit in the bottom for en-
trance ; when rigged, they are like an oblong
box, and, naturally, after you have got in and
arranged your blankets, getting out again is a
work of time and difficulty. One thing is, that
although you can see out, no one can well
see in, unless there is a light behind the net.
G woke up suddenly that night, although
he was not aware of any sound having aroused
him. He lay for some time listening, when he
was conscious of some strange objects being visible
underneath the edge of the tarpaulin. It was
about full moon, and there was no doubt of what
the objects were : the legs of about seven or eight
blackfellows. Presently one looked cautiously in
at the end opening. G had been lying on his
back with one knee up ; unthinkingly he put his
leg down. The blackfellow, having the bunk
between himself and the other moonlit opening,
could see the movement, and instantly dropped
behind some bags of flour. G lay perfectly
still and waited, getting his revolver ready. Pre-
sently the nigger got up and cautiously advanced
club in hand. Once within the shadow of the
tarpaulin he could see nothing inside the mosquito-
net, although G. could see him plainly. Noise-
lessly he crept on until at last he was trying to
peer through the net, quite unconscious that he
was almost looking down the muzzle of G.'s pistol,
who settled matters by pulling the trigger. There
was a great clatter outside, and the wounded man
made a dive and scramble under the edge of the
tarpaulin and disappeared. The men, awakened
by the shot, came out of the tents, and one of them
hearing a noise in the river-bed went down the
bank and almost tumbled over the nigger crawling
on his hands and knees and coughing gouts of
blood up, for the bullet had gone into his mouth
and down his throat. G. had set his net on fire
when he fired the shot and was busy putting it out.
When he went outside, they picked up eight big
two-handed clubs that the blacks had dropped
during their hasty flight. It was the greatest
start they had had for some time.

THE BEALE PATENT
HIGH ARM SEWING MACHINES
Have, by their continued merits and the general
satisfaction they have invariably given, won the
first place in public esteem, and are on all sides
admitted to be the best and most useful sewing
machines in the market.
EOR USE IN THE HOUSEHOLD
The Beale Machine presents innumerable advant-
ages. The mechanism and the' adjustments art-
perfect, while the working is most simple; and
the addition of all the most recent inventions and
improvements renders the manipulation of every-
thing connected with the machine a matter of
perfect ease. The result of this is, that with
ordinary care the machine will last for many
years, nothing can get out of order, and no time
is wasted in tiresome meddling with the parts.
It is a servant that will never grumble, never
rebel, never want nights out, willing even on
washing days. It only asks for a careful cleaning
now and then, a snug, dry corner and a little
genuine sowing machine oil. Any other oil will
make it sick, and dirt of all kind it abhors.
The Self-threading Shuttle, of solid steel,
saves the trouble of threading by the end of the
cotton. It has an excellent tension, is strong and
simple.
The Bobbin Winder is a marvel of in-
genuity, winding and guiding the cotton by
itself, as evenly as upon the reel whence it came,
and stopping of its own accord when the bobbin
is full.
For all kinds of family sewing and fancy the
Beale Machine has the greatest capacity.
T.. e Improved High Arm. A great de-
sideratum with ladies has been the raising of the
arm of the machine, so as to allow the goods to be
sewn freely to pass underneath. This had to bo
accomplished without increasing the weight of the
machine. In fact, the Beale runs with wonderful
ease and rapidity. It is almost silent, all clicking
sounds having been obviated. As is well known,
a good test of the finish of any mechanism is its
silence when in rapid motion.
With the Wonderful Beale Machine,
whether for hand or foot use, you can hem any
width, make double seams or fells, braid stitch
with chenille, Berlin wool, or gold cord, and Such
like fancy work ; yon may make a pretty lace
stitch, tuck ever so finely, bind dresses, coats, or
carpets ; sew on trimmings without guiding, make
frilling, ruffling, or ruenmg; quilt, weave your
own braid with any combination of colours and
stitch it on at the same time ; make thick, woollen
door-mats, embroidered antimacassars, window
curtains, and so forth ; you can easily and rapidly
perform the fashionable shirring or gathering so
popular for sleeves, bodies, or skirts of dresses
with an appliance supplied gratis ; lay the small
cords in hoods and stays ; do large cording ; gather
with or without a band, and generally do all
kinds of straight sewing upon any material what-
ever. You can darn upon any woven goods, and
do crewel-work. The Beale Patent will pass
from eight folds of tweed or twenty of calico to
two of thinnest muslin, or even mosquito-net or
tulle, without the operator changing needle,
cotton, stitch, or tension, and the fine material
will not be gathered in the slightest. Yon may
make button and eyelet-holes, do over-casting,
make herring-bone stitch upon any materials.
This work is absolutely unique and can only be
accomplished upon the Beale Patent Machine.
Any of these performances will bo shown at our
shops at any time. The Beale Patent makes
a lock-stitch, which cannot ravel, yet by a momen-
tary alteration the sewing can be done so as to
rip from end to end, if desired. This peculiarity
is sometimes useful when children’s clothes are
intended to be altered.
Nickel-plating’. —ln our climate the exposed
parts of the machine, and the applieances, need
to be protected from rust. We have, at consider-
able expense, added a handsomely nickeled wheel,
nickeled plates, screws, appliances, treadle-bar
and cones. Heightening the appearance, these
improvements also tend largely to preserve the
parts. This plating is imperishable.

The London Times has at length realised
that Australia is a country of some importance,
and now publishes regularly a special telegram
from Dalziel, giving the principal Australian
news of the day.
According to Secretary Knapp, of the
Local Option League, N.S.W. spent in drink
during 1882-90, <£39,760,997, or more than it has
altogether spent in State railways, and more than
the value of all the gold yet extracted from its
soil.
At a recent Melbourne sale of unredeemed
pledges, an old lady made a bid—the only one—
of Is. for a large family Bible. “ What,” quoth
the auctioneer, “Is. for a .£5 Bible!” “Oh,”
replied the old lady, in a matter-of-fact way, “the
Bible’s gone out of date ; nobody reads it now.”
George Houston Reid, leader of the
N.S.W. Opposition, was no end of a cricketer
some score of years ago, and on one occasion
played with his office, the Treasury, against the
Registrar-General’s Office. The R.G.’s stayed in
the whole afternoon, and late in the evening
George was put on to bowl as a last resource,
“ h ow then, gentlemen, we have him,” said
George, affixing his eyeglass, and he delivered his
first ball, which passed some six feet over the bats-
man’s head, and was duly no-balled.
An Adelaide writer tells this about the late
Major O’Halloran, who owned O’Halloran Hill,
Mount Barker way, S.A. Near the boundary of
his land was a permanent well, much used by
travellers. What “riled” the Major was that
teamsters frequently left the slip-rails down, thus
making stock liable to stray. At last, being
determined to shut off the supply, he erected a
notice-board, bearing the legend “ Major O’Hal-
loran’s well.” When next he visited it he found
that some waggish drover had elaborately added a
postscript : “Glad to hear it. Hope Mrs.
O’Halloran’s well, too.”

Tapling, M.P., has left to the Britiah
Museum his unique collection of postage-stamps,

Father Anderledy, Jesuit Chief, director
of 12,500 picked men stationed all over the world,
is dead. The Review of Reviews recently gave
a portrait of Anderledy, a horribly grim-looking,
lean, tall man.
A Sydney doctor made an awkward mis-
take the other day. There was a little girl among
his patients, and she lived in one of these terraces
where every house is built exactly alike. There-
fore the doctor, being in a hurry, dashed into the
wrong house, charged upstairs to the bed-room,
found another little girl in bed there, and seeing
that she was evidently convalescent gave her half
a glassful of evil-smelling medicine and went off.
When he was gone the infant aroused the house
with her shrieks and explained the outrage which
a strange man had perpetrated upon her, and her
father ran 300 yards or so in pursuit of the doctor
with a large poker, but luckily failed to overtake
him. This is only one of the many evils of the
terrace system. It is related on unreliable
authority that another Sydney surgeon was so
much deceived once by the awful similarity of
terrace houses that he cut off the leg of the man
next door by mistake, but the error was hushed up
to save the credit of the profession.
Dear Bulletin, —It amuses me to see
statistics quoted. As an Excise officer I had for
four or five years to collect the agricultural returns
for a large English district. The system was to
send out schedules by post to the occupiers, who
were supposed to fill in and return. Anyone
acquainted with the average Hodge knows how
this was done. The work had to be got through
in a few weeks—as many months were really
necessary—and I received a dole of £6 from the
Board of Trade, whereas were the thing properly
performed £100 would be too little. Officials high
and low regarded the thing as a farce, and our
instructions were to strike a balance anyhow, and
get through as quickly as possible. An Excise
inspector once publicly declared that the agricul-
tural returns were not worth the paper on which
they were printed. How many schedules have I
filled in with my left hand —occasionally adding
“donkeyin the column for horses to give an air
of realism to Hodge’s supposed return? A few
months after the returns were published and
every paper in the kingdom had a leading article
on the country’s agricultural progress. How the
2000 Excise officers who collected them sniggered !
—Yours,
Ex-Tax-Collector.
IT RECALLS THE BLIZZARD.
In March, 1888, the great blizzard in America
almost extinguished the city of New York. The
like of it was never before seen. The snow com-
pletely stopped all local traffic. Not a horse or a
wheel could move. All the telegraph lines lead-
ing into the city were prostrated, and for two or
three days the people of Boston and New York
communicated with each other by way of London
by means of the Atlantic cables. Thus messages
intended for three hundred miles distant were
sent six thousand miles, crossing the ocean twice.
An incident which recalls this experience to the
writer’s mind happened a short time ago here in
England. Suppose we let the gentleman in-
terested tell his own story. He says: “I have
suffered more or less from indigestion and
dyspepsia all my life. I had a bad taste in the
mouth, pain after eating, a poor appetite, and
sour stomach. My tongue was coated, and my
mouth constantly filled with a watery fluid. No
matter what I ate, however light, it disagreed
with me and gave me pain. I had fulness of the
chest, and pain at my side, with a miserable, low,
dull feeling. From time to time I consulted a
doctor who gave me medicines, but they did very
little good. The doctor said that the coating of
my stomach was disordered, and the mucous
membrane was inflamed. In 1877 I received a
pamphlet from New York telling of a medicine
called Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup, and of the
extraordinary cures it had effected, so I procured
a supply, and after taking the first I felt better,
and by the time I had taken four bottles more, I
was completely cured, and have been in good
health, as regards the indigestion, ever since.
Having studied the practice of herbs for many
years, 1 was in the habit of treating people for
erysipelas and other complaints, and was fre-
quently consulted by people in the district, so
after my remarkable cure, I was so impressed
with the merits of Seigel’s Syrup, that I procured
a large supply of the medicine, and recommended
it to all who were suffering, and people came from
far and near for it. I may mention that on a
Sunday ray house was beseiged by miners from
Coal Pit Heath, and others from a distance. On
every hand I heard nothing but the loudest praise
of it and of the cures it effected, and the fame of
this medicine was spread throughout the West of
England with no other advertising than one party
telling another of the benefit they had derived
from this wonderful medicine. • I wish every one
to know of this, and if by publishing this state-
ment it will help others who may be suffering as I
was, it will afford me pleasure.”
The letter from which the foregoing is an
extract is signed Moses Godwin, Old Sqdbury
(Sodbury), Glos., and is dated April 9th, 1891.
He is a farmer.
The reader will notice that while the head-
quarters for the sale of Mother Seigel’s Syrup is
universally known to be in London, by a strange
chance Mr. Godwin’s first information concerning
it came from America, three thousand miles away,
which recalls the incident of the American blizzard
above narrated, and also shows that the fame and
usefulness of this medicine extend to all civilised
countries. ' *
Mr. Benjamin Edgerton, grocer and provision
dealer, Plat Lane, Whixall, Whitchurch, Salop,
says: “While living with Mr. Roberts, Fens
Woods Farm, I first began to feel a .dull, heavy
weight at my side, and noticed a bad taste in the
mouth with foul stomach and uncomfortable feel-
ing after eating. I had n6 appetite, and when I
sat down to the table I could not touch the food.
X had a good deal of pain and noise in the head,
and could not sleep for it. .1 was not fit for heavy
work, and could only do light jobs abput the
farm. After cutting a hedge I would go quite
faint, and had to. sit down, and felt so much
depressed I could have cried. Having always
been such a strong man I took it badly to be
reduced to such a weak state. I took all kinds of
physic and saw a doctor, but his medicine only
eased me; for a bit, and then. I was. worse than
before. I went on in this way for over a year,
when a servant that came to live at Mr. Roberts
told me of a medicine called Mother Seigel’s
Syrup. She had heard a gentleman talking about
it in thq’railway carriage, and he praised it so
much that I thought I would try it. After I had
taken two bottles fhy food did me good, and I
gained strength, and by persevering with the
syrup I soon got as strong as ever, and have never
ailed anything since.”
In Memoriam.
CARDINAL MANNING, 1808—1892.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Another noble spirit passed away 1
Whose sympathies disdained to be suppressed
By creed or social statute. Of the oppressed
True champion 1 ever foremost in the fray,
When Right, down-trodden, strove to hold at bay
The greedy tyrant. One who, much caressed
In brightest places, ne’er to cry distressed
Turned a deaf ear, or said the suppliant nay!
In fullness of his time he gains the prize,
And claims his,well-earned diadem of grace ;
And England, grief-bowed, stands, with tear-dimmed eyes,
Seeking if there be one to fill his place
And worthily uphold, in all men’s sight,
His glorious standard, “ God defend the right.”
Another warrior-priest has joined his peers !
That eagle-vision, which nought here could sate,
Has pierced the mystery beyond life’s gate,
And found the haven often sought with tears.
His was a fearless nature. Through long years
He gave Right his allegiance ; consecrate
To one high purpose, self to subjugate
And serve his God, as did the prophet-seers.
Leaders of thought die not. It is their high
And proud prerogative, sun-crowned, to stand
Fronting the ages. Time will pass them by
And not efface their footprints from the strand;
But in our hearts their best memorial lives
And to our lives a nobler impulse gives.
Melbourne, 15/l/’92.
A.V.G.
“ For 30 years this remote and solitary
city was his home.”—London Black and White
on Bishop Perry and Marvellous Melbourne.
Nob only was Cardinal Manning a widower,
but he had two children, who died in infancy.
Here are facts which throw a light on the causes
of his efficiency and sympathy.
The polite people of Bathurst, N.S. W.,
don’t say of a traveller “ Redraws the long bow„”
but “ How long is his snake? ” They merely ask
the question. The seven-foot snake which i£ four
feet long by standard measure is familiar to every
bushman.

Trades and Labour Council, asks us to give notice
to Michael Knight and his brother Thomas,
bricklayers (present address unknown), who were
in Perth five or six years ago and worked at one
time in Guildford, that two blocks of land belong-
ing to the former and now becoming valuable have
been fenced in by some land-shark who evidently
intends to jump them.

We have read “Nora, or a Strange Re-
demption,” by Mary Angela Dickens—the grand-
daughter of the great novelist; we have also read
a number of opinions by critical wiseacres who
hail the young novelist as one upon whom her
great progenitor’s mantle has fallen. The story is
clever, but not especially well-written, and no
more indicates an inheritance of genius than
Richard Cromwell's abortive efforts at state-craft
evidenced consanguinity with the mighty Lord-
Protector.
It seems pretty clear that Dr. Mueller, of
Victoria (not the Baron), has established the
success of his (now that the rationale of the treat-
ment is demonstrated) obviously commonsense
treatment, by liquor-strychnise injection, of
snake-bite. Should further tests uphold the
efficacy of the Mueller treatment, it will be alike
the privilege and the duty of the Australian
Governments to recognise by a handsome honora-
rium the splendid service conferred upon humanity
by this unobtrusive German scientist. Apropos,
somebody writes to the papers singing the praises
of eucalyptus as a snake-bite remedy. The fact
that the aborigines don’t" use eucalyptus argues
strongly against this theory.
Editor Bulletin, Sydney,—Allow me to
take exception to a statement in your last, to the
effect that “ Grandmaster Powderly, of the
Knights of Labour, is a cast-iron Protectionist,
anyhow.” If his own utterances are to be relied
on, he is nothing of the kind, for it is only a short
time ago since he wrote, in the Knights of
Labour Journal : “ It seems to me that the policy
of Protection is a wrong upon labour. The
labouring classes are not protected—only the
manufacturers. The rich receive the protection.
The American manufacturer adds the duty to
what should be the natural and fair price of his
product. The burden of the tariff rests its crush-
ing weight 'upon the poor. It is the poor, the
great mass of the people, who need protection,
and they will never get it under the so-called
policy of Protection.” The above does notread
like an extract from a Protectionist speech, and,
as a matter of fact, Powderly, instead of being
an advocate of Protection, is an out-and-out
Single-taxer.—Yours, &c., J.C., Windsor, Jan. 24.

RUNNING THE ASSASSINS DOWN.
The brace of dark-skinned fiends who so merci-
lessly despatched the unfortunate foreigner,
Mursezvitz, at Dora Dora, on the 2nd May last,
for the purpose of possessing themselves of the
little money and goods he had scraped together
by plodding industry and self-denial, have con-
tinued so far to keep a safe distance between their
pursuers and themselves, and this, too, notwith-
standing the
PROMPT AND HOT PURSUIT
of the officers of justice in their very tracks.
Through stretches of wild country, now on the
trail, then off it, over swollen creeks, through
almost impassable brushwood, across mountain-
ridges, or threading an uncertain course under the
gloom of towering trees in the bewildering
primeval forest, the messengers commissioned to
act a prominent part in clearing up
A CRUEL CRIME
have hunted these outlawed aborigines without
success. Once or twice hope dawned, but only to
be crushed in its creation. The wild character of
the country formed the savages’ shield, until just
when fears were merging into despair that the
murderers had escaped for ever from the grip of
earthly justice, the news was brought that
THEY HAD BEEN SEEN
in the vicinity of Tamworth, and still later near
the Macleay. A remarkable incident connected
with the search was the discovery in one of the
most lonesome spots visited by the police of the
remains of what had been a camp fire. This, it
was at first believed, might afford a clue to the
whereabouts of the fugitives. Closer examination,
however, showed that the camp had not been that
of a blackfellqw, while the finding close by of a
bottle that had recently contained St. Jacobs Oil,
the great pain conqueror and bushman's friend,
left
NO ROOM FOR DOUBT
but that a prudent swagsman had halted at the
spot. The immense value of St. Jacobs Oil to
persons much exposed to the elements is widely
recognised ; while the simplicity of its application,
together with the wonderful curative qualities it
possesses in all cases of rheumatism, neuralgia,
sprains, gout, bruises, &c., renders it a valuable
adjunct to the labourer, the farmer, and the
squatter.

“ Three Hundred a Year”: A Politico-
Social Study.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The difference between an ordinary citizen
n( j a member of Parliament is the difference
between sweet insignificance and Damnable
think that a member’s life is all
Grlory and Glou-Glou.
Bah!
Pride hurled Satan into Hell; Envy, the
Mother of Pride, sucks men into Parliament.
The result is the same to the victims.
# * *
Notice the town member, how he goes ;
be smiles not, neither doth he grin—if you could
rea d the dark and troubled sea of emotions that
ddle up beneath his ample white waistcoat.
His house, or shop, or office, is the rendezvous of
hundreds of perfect strangers, who call him
familiarly by name, tell him how hard they fought
to get him returned, worry him with their private
woes, rush him off to dark-corner prayer and
other meetings, and borrow his shillings lavishly.
He has to have seven different addresses each
week if he wants any time to himself; has to whip
into Parliament House with an unexpected rush
at all sorts of odd moments from the cavern of a
cab in order to be present at any debate, for he
dare not remain there in daylight, except when
preconcerted signals and instructions have been
arranged for. Where he sleeps is a mystery.
# * *
But the woes of the town member are
nothing as compared with those of the country
member.
A country member must live at an hotel, or
have a family house, and he must have an office
where telegrams and letters from his constituents
may easily reach him. Woe be to the country
member who fails to reply at once to any
“collect” wire from a distant supporter—who,
no matter how insignificant abroad, is always a
“ prominent citizen ” in his own township, and
can call up local hurricanes at will.
A knowing country member should —and often
(j oes _have two offices ; a secret one to which he
retreats whenever his chances let him; and an
advertised one, where he is never found, and
where he keeps a faithful clerk to tell lies for him,
and to let him know things ; or, if he can’t afford
a faithful clerk, there is always to be found a
door-notice —“Back in about 20 minutes.”
It is well to be careful of the “about,” for it
makes the constituents, or deputation, doubtful
when they return from a first call whether the
member hasn’t been in and gone again while they
dallied with town delights. But, in any case, a
member can always afterwards explain that a
thousand pressing public duties bullock-hauled
him away just at that particular time.
A country member’s hotel, or private house, is
never safe against intruding friends from the
wilds, which made him what he is.
If I were a country member of say, the N.S.W.
Parliament, I should like to be the member for
the Soudan, Africa I might get a little peace in
that situation. *
The country visitor to town regards his parti-
cular member in the light of a bank, in which he
is a shareholder, and from which he can draw
sovereigns at call on the strength of I 0 U’s.
Country deputations regard visits to the
metropolis as being made up of an hour’s sober
interview with a Minister of the Crown, and
three days’ wild orgy with their member as their
pilot, or, at least, their stand-by.
One beetle-browed ruffian will swoop upon his
country member just outside his hotel, and calling
up three or four more beetle-browed ruffians in
painfully new slop suits, he will airily hiccough
with a comprehensive arm-sweep round the whole
horizon—
“ My frens, Misshur Basham, meramer for
Shoggeroggera (hie) ; Misshur Basham, shese are
four o’ your mosh hard-working shporters ” (hie).
This always happens just at lunch-time, and
Basham, M.P., understands his duty too well not
to invite them in, first to drink and then to eat.
Overcome by the splendour of their surroundings
and fearing to offend their member after his
graciousness, these intruders “wolf” religiously
through the bill of fare, item by item, from top to
bottom, from oysters even unto coffee. When
asked what they will drink they always say “a
small bottle.”
I know a member whose last four bashful
lunohers cost him just £4 16s.
* » #
When the country member visits his
native heath these same beetle-browed consti-
tuents are too humble to presume to ask so great
and distinguished a man to grace their humble
boards; they know their proper places too well
for that!
No, they visit him at the Joggeroggera Hotel,
and as he is again their host, the hotel becomes
an open house for them. Moreover, as the local
police wink at the mighty local member—for hath
he not influence towards promotion?—the land-
lord is not particular about the law, but takes
unusual privileges as to hours, &c., and the hotel
is practically a pandemonium all night, from
which there is no escape for the country member.
Should he try to dodge his admirers by going to
bed at eleven o’clock, they swarm with un-
diminished enthusiasm into his bedroom, light the
candle, sit down upon his bed, and drunkenly
talk him into a cold sweat.
He endures it for the least possible period com-
patible with duty, and so soon as he can get the
editor of the local paper insensibly alcoholised,
and his buggy out of the frantic hands of his loving
constituents, he flies swiftly back to the city.
* * *
But though his constituents whom he
thus leaves behind cannot reach him by hand-
grasp, they can by telegraph.
Herein he lies completely at their mercy. They
send him “ collect ” messages in “ loose-extension ”
form, quite indifferent that they are expressing in
47 words what might have been said with equal
felicity and intelligibility in seven. Their trouble !
He’s a “ member,” and he gets £3OO a year from
the bleeding country, and “ we got him the billet!”
So he curses, and pays for the ‘‘ collect ” messages;
and looks pleasant, and pays for the necessary
replies.
This little item of telegrams, with postage-
stamps added (stationery is provided at Parlia-
ment House), will cost the most ordinary country
member at least £1 a week—unless he is a very
clever man; some it costs as much as £3 per
week, half their Parliamentary salary.
Watty Adams, M.L.A. for Bundaberg, Queens-
land —and I don’t see why Bananaland should be
more expensive in these lines than N.S.W.—
assured me once that for many weeks his tele-
graphic expenses amounted to from £0 to £l5 per
week ! 1 interpolate this because when I heard it,
I thought the statement excessive; yet Watty is a
sober and truthful man.
• * •
It must not be supposed that the
“collect ” telegrams from the constituents always,
or even often, refer to public business. Quite the
opposite. The country member is the constituents’
bank, whenever they choose to fancy that they
have anything to tell him.
For instance, Constituent Spudriver wires
down some information most important to himself,
and Basham gets a telegraphic envelope marked —
“ Collect, wds., .”
He pays the money, opens the telegram and
reads:
To Basil Basham, Esq., M.L.A.
Hotel , Sydney.
I am going to get married next Thursday to Lucy Ann
Downcreek, and we come to Sydney for the honeymoon.
I want you to engage us rooms somewhere—slap-up style
and no flies. You remember her, flue girl, with flashing
black eyes, and grand figure. She, what danced so well
at the Selectors’ Ball, time you was there. First met her
time I was canvassing for yon, through the Round Scrub.
Will wire you again what train to meet us by.
J. Spudrivef.
This telegram means two levies on Basham,
M.P.—(l) A wedding present; (2) engaging ex-
pensive apartments in his own name.
He goes and does the latter at once, and, in
doing it, runs several risks, among which are (1)
that the bride-elect may throw over J. Spudriver
in the meantime ; (2) that the bridegroom may get
so drunk that, by the time he reaches the city, he
has to be nursed through the jimjams at Basham’s
expense.
The above telegram is very similar to one sent
two years ago to a country member. The subject
was a honeymoon, and the country member had
to pay for the honeymoon residence and press a
cheque into the bride’s hand (she was the wife of
the chairman of his Cow Flat committee) instead
of himself selecting a bridal gift for her. It only
cost him £32 11s,, not counting the telegrams and
the law-costs, for the hotelkeeper had to sue him
to get the money.
* * *
Then the keen-nosed country constituent
wires to his member :
A son is born unto us. Have you any objection to my
naming the boy after you ? I voted for you several times,
and have a great admiration of your character so far.
The boy is a fine fellow, fully 141b5., and I hope the child
will grow’ up to be a conscientious and useful citizen and
an honour and glory to his country, which he will if he
takes you for a pattern. Collect.
The member wires back :
No objection; proud to be thought of; congratulate
you.
Then, when the boy’s full name is known, the
member has to find a silver mug for, and stand
fairy godfather to, him. Lots of people find it
far cheaper to be own father to a child than god-
father.
* * #
Then the happy father has a childless
neighbour, who sees and ponders over these things.
He is envious, and lets the matter worry him.
At last, a bright idea arrives.
He owns a dog / So he wires straightway to the
godfather member :
I have named my dog after you ; please forward brass
collar, with your name in full inscribed upon it. You
know the dog—a black-and-white, woolly-haired sort of
collie cattle-dog ; four years old now, so you know what
sized collar. Collect.
The collar is sent!
Now, the member who is the victim of this
dog imposition happens to have a melodious, dis-
syllabic, tongue-handy name that is not a misfit
for a high-toned canine. He was telling a couple
of pressmen about it, when one said
“Well, it isn’t every member who has a name
that would fit a dog!”
Icicles were precipitated on the spot.
# # *
Your pietist is the expert at running a
cheap election. They dew say that Alfred Allen,
the N.S.W. temperance and insurance man, is a
perfect master in the art of getting returned on
the strength of his many and great, though by us
unseen, virtues.
The late David Buchanan, of N.S.W., was pro-
verbial for cheap and nasty electioneering.
Davvvid once stood for Mudgee, while a church
bazaar was being promoted. An ordinarily
squeezable member cannot hope to get rid of a
bazaar canvasser under two or three guineas.
David, in his capacity as next member was
approached on bazaar business by a fetching young
lady.
“ And what church dae ye rapresent, ma dear
girl ?” enquired Dawicf.
“ Presbyterian,” she artfully replied, thinking
David was o’ the kirk.
“ Ma dear chiel,” said David in his most im-
pressive and grandiloquent tone, “ I don’t believe
in ony churches instituted by Man. Ma church is
the Church of Nature, an’ nae ither, where I
staun’ under the blue, o’erarching canopy o’
Heeven, an’ drink ma fill. As I gaze upon the
gigantic an’ bauttlemented crags, an’ hear the crash
an’ the reverberation o’ the thunner, God’s own
awe-inspirin’ artillery, I feel ma sowl respond to
its graund anthems, an’ I feel ma hairt lifted
up far, far beyond the most magneefioent cathe-
dral that’s but b’ human haundiwark. Ah ! ma
dear chiel, believe me, the releegion o’ the a’-en-
during ages is the releegion o’ the Church o’
Nature.”
And not a domned cent would Dawvid give !
• * *
John Hurley, once of Queensland, was
contesting Central Cumberland (N.S.W.) when
he had plenty of shekels. He instructed his elec-
tion committee “ to go to any reasonable extent ”
in anything which they thought would advance
his electioneering interests. They took him at
his word.
A day or two afterwards he was startled in his
hotel apartments by a hideous row. He went
upon the balcony and saw a drag attached to a
magnificent team of four white horses, a brass
band, and a cheering and frantic crowd. With a
deep, unheard curse of disgust he drew back into
the apartment, drawing with him his wife, who
had followed him to the balcony.
“Oh!” exclaimed Madame, woman-like, plea-
surably excited at the spectacle—“oh ! John, do
go out and show yourself. These people evi-
dently think a lot of you !”
Those people thought so much of John that he
received from them, a few days later, a bill for
£35, as the charge for the drag and for the band's
expenses, “ including lunches !” A brass bands-
man’s lunch—likewise his lunch-bill —is an awe-
some thing.
That election cost John Hurley over .£llOO.
* * *
John also contested Lithgow, but this
time he hadn’t a million to spend. Nevertheless,
he was successful.

tion committees formed a mutual alliance for the

“ We have got a young widow over here, Mr.

Mr. Hurley hinted that, under the circum-

* * *
I here remember that a candidate once
tried to boom his election on a reputation for phil-
anthropy. He headed a subscription for buying
a mangle for a needy widow ; ho raised the
money, bought the mangle, and his chances looked
promising. But some noser found out that he
took the new mangle to his own home, and sent
the widow his own old one ! That killed his can-
didature, and he has had to lie low politically
ever since, though it is an unswerving article of
faith with his son that his dad was only beaten
“because he promised to poll-tax Irishmen.”
* # *
Do politics pay % It depends on the man.
With a generous man who has naught else to
depend on, the ,£3OO a year means ruin. But
where do the counterpoising perks come in ? It is
said, and I believe it, that, since Labor has
organised and developed its fighting strength,
Labor men can run their elections cheaply. But,
when they are once safely elected do they get
“ milked” as the moneyed members do, or did ? If
so, it’s good-bye to all chance of their effecting
strong work. A man who is “ bled white” must
be amenable to insidiously offered chances of
earning money apparently not dishonourably.
And then ? Conscience weakens always towards
its possessors, and a poor man so placed must
gradually develope into a “ professional Conserva-
tive.”
THE DIPSOMANIAC.
The man who sends us this will yet either
be on the Bench or behind the spikes- you see
almost exactly the same sort of men in both
places - .
Editor Bulletin,— At Maclean (N.S.W.)a man named
Smith shot at a kanaka, but so unreliable was his aim
that he injured a little girl, and now two charges are
brought against him—viz., shooting with intent and
inflicting grievous bodily harm. The grievous bodily
harm must be regarded by law as purely an accident, for
if it is proven that Smith aimed for the kanaka, to charge
him with shooting the little girl is as reasonable as it
would be to arraign him likewise for attempted suicide,
had the pistol discharged at the wrong end and done
business with Smith. Again, if the charge of grievous
bodily harm is of any account-, that of bloodthirsty atten-
tions to the kanaka must be wrongly laid. Now, how
would the case go, supposing Smith went out, gun in
hand, with the expressed intention of killing somebody
else, and by mistake aimed at the kanaka, still fetching
the little girl ? In this case there can be only one charge
against the man Smith ; either that of shooting with
intent, or inflicting grievous bodily harm ; but the most
rational charge to prefer would be that of “ missing the
kanaka”—that is to say, “ hitting the little girl."’—Yours,
&c., Solon.
The Awakening.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
They had lived together for seven years, and were
more or less married “after God’s holy ordinance ”
—more or less because, although all formalities had
been complied with, the Church obeyed, and
social laws subscribed to, yet the Divine consum-
mation of •“ one flesh ” was a dream only that died
witli the echo of the marriage peal.
The end of the first month brought the awaken-
ing—a word, a misunderstanding, a sneer, a
retort —and the two were two again, the dream
over, and they faced eacli other across the bottom-
less abyss of a fatal dissimilarity. They did not
quarrel—it was bad form ; nor had they time, so
busy were they in fortifying themselves in the
little castle of their own regrets and decorating
the walls thereof with pictures of what might have
been ; under one roof, and yet so far apart; within
the same four walls, and yet in different
worlds; of the same name, husband and wife, and
yet unrelated ; but the world knew nothing of it.
Harry Walford’s perfect form and absolute
politeness to his wife was, among their friends, a
perennial topic of conversation, and her complete
coincidence with his every wish made other men
envious. “ They are so confoundedly civil to each
other, they really might be mere acquaintances, 5 '
people said —the fact that they were so was the
explanation. With all this they were neverthe-
less quite above the level of the common herd of
men and women, two distinct and well-marked
personalities—electro-plated, the one with an
amalgam of commerce and domestic reticence,
the other with a cursed sense of inappreciation :
they sat, fed, slept together, and sometimes
kissed, but the whole thing was bloodless,
metallic, of the earth earthy. They lived as it were
in a fog of unreality, through which the souls of
both of them still peered hopefully for some bright
morning dawn of helpful and heartful companion-
ship.
One day brought the unexpected news of great
wealth left them, and they sat the evening
through in her boudoir and talked of luxuries to
come —horses, carriages, a town and country
house and a cottage on the river—to all she gave
a hollow acquiescence, but told herself, as he left
the room, that life would still be empty for her,
for “love was all, and wealth was nought,” and
he, as he mounted the stairs, was telling himself
the same sickening certainty.
In one particular this unhappy pair stood alone,
there was in the case of neither of them any
haunting memory of a first love, a mad passion
unrequited ; they met, admired, married, and
risked the mating, and the mating never came—
that was all.
That night she went early to bed, and he, still
dressed, went to her room half determined to talk
to her for once from his heart and of his thirst for
a real union of souls after the manner of God’s
cementing; so drawing her low chair to the
fire he threw himself into it and wrestled with
himself for a while, and in the end compromised
matters by speaking unto her in parables. He
told her of himself through the disguise of an
imaginary friend of his—an awfully good chap,
with the warmest heart and strongest hand to do
great things in the world —move mountains and
stir people, if only he had some woman to love
him, some earnest, clinging, trusting one who
would invite him to be at once “a man and fold
her with an arm, a God and hold her with a
charm.” And she, listening with the eyes, ears
and.mouth other, had slowly risen from her pillow
on to one elbow to ask :
“ Is he married? ”
“ Yes,” the reply came slowly, “ to an iceberg.”
For half-an-hour he talked on and on, and never
before had she known the better part of him—the
silent half —grow eloquent after this fashion ; and
she, too, for the first time tasted something of
life’s possibilities, all kinds of thoughts in her
struggled for utterance. Conventionality fought
with kindliness, the loyal wife with a tender
womanhood, and victory passed culture by and
crowned the Innate. Then falling back on the
pillow she groaned out—“ Oh ! that I could help
this man—write to him, or, or .”
“You may write to him.”
“But would it not be against every conven-
tional law? What would the world say?—what
will you say ?”
“ As far as I am concerned I would sacrifice
anything to see my friend happy, and as for social
laws they have always stood in the way of God’s
commands and have been sacrificed.”
And so the matter was arranged and the corres-
pondence started, and her letters, full of a holy
love and a yearning to be of help to him, went to
the club addressed to “ Chas. Terrain,” and her
husband read them there and had them answered
on club paper.
The subject after the first day was never men-
tioned, but the whole atmosphere of home life
was slowly changing, a late spring was breaking
in upon the winter of their discontent. There
were handsqueezings, evening walks, and real
kissings now.
A month over and they were man and wife
indeed —one flesh, but for on her side the hideous
consciousness of the fact that she was still carry-
ing on a correspondence that she had neither the
right nor the wish to continue. “ Why should she
share with another a love that her husband was
now, by every law of God and man, entitled to
in its entirety? Would she ever be in a position
to return his constant glance of absolute trust and
confidence?” were questions she asked herself
daily, and prayed each night that soon it might
be so.
One day she made her resolve, and with a
woman’s impetuosity sat down there and then to
carry it out. She wrote to this “ Charles Jerram ”
and asked him as a great favour to grant her one
meeting, and hear from her own lips why the
thing should end.
I will be at the Albert Memorial, beneath the chestnuts
on the right, at 10.30 to-morrow (she wrote). Will you
meet me there, wearing a red carnation? This corres-
pondence, commenced so strangely, has been, you are
good enough to say, helpful to you ; to me it has been
more so than you can ever guess. Why should itcontinue?
Two travellers in a mountainous country have met where
a glacier yawned, and given eaeli other a hand over ; what
remains but to say “ good-bye? ”
Five minutes before the time appointed she was
there on the seat and endeavouring to form some
sort of picture of the man she was about to meet.
Would he be nice and easy to talk to, she won-
dered ; like his letters—open, candid, and yet so
respectful; or would he, as he easily might, take
advantage of her loneliness and the strange posi-
tion in which she was placed? This last possi-
bility came in upon her with a rush and sickened
her. The trees and the ground beneath her feet
swam round and round ; she took a hasty glance
at the clock, and, seeing that there were still two
minutes before the half-hour, she snatched up her
bunch of flowers and dived into the dense scrub
that separated her from the Serpentine. She was
barely out of sight when a.step-a firm, heavy
step —close to her, brought her to a standstill.
She looked up and there, within reach of her
hand, stood her husband, wearing the red carna-
tion.
Then the whole truth came in upon her with a
rush, and with a little scream that had no fear in
it, she fell into her rightful resting place.
MANNINGTON CAFFYN.
Dear Bulletin, — The Colonial Institute
is not without its uses, as a place where members
can make artless confession of the faith that they
have instituted. Sir James Crichton-Browne
whose connection with the kawlinies is somewhat
mysterious and uncertain, has been telling his
bogus Australian friends that, as he casts his eye
across the seas and observes the expansion of
universities in these primitive regions, he can’t ■
help feeling some regret. Regret not so much
because ignorance is always favorable to intense
loyalty, but for the reason that such expansion
may tend to reduce the emigration of young
Australians to Oxford and Cambridge, where they
acquire that “love of England which must j
materially strengthen the bonds,” &c. Crichton- ,
Browne, bless him ! never thinks of looking at
the other side of this great international question.
Why can’t the gilded youth of Great Britain try a
course of education at Sydney or Melbourne
University, and return home with a boundless
affection for Australia and a supreme contempt
for the Institute where it is misrepresented?
A young Australian must be schooled in English
fallacies and saturated with English customs,
ideas, prejudices and snobbery before he can
want any bonds at all, and can swallow
the awkward circumstance that the bonds are a
purely English arrangement which we are con-
tinually exhorted to strengthen at the same old
end—our end.—E. E.
One of the saddest things on record was
the sight of the six editors of the six evening
papers in Melbourne, Sydney, and Brisbane all
sitting in their offices the other Thursday evening,
and waiting—waiting like six ghouls for theDuke of
Clarence to die —they thought he was going to die,
for he had pneumonia and his temperature was up
to 104. They had six leading articles all written
up and ready, stating that the world was a sombre
blank now that Clarence was dead, and six more
stating that all nature had brightened up since he
had recovered, and yet he wouldn’t either die nr
recover in any definite way. iSo they prowled
round with their articles, and swore be-
cause the prince wouldn’t die in time for the
four o’clock edition ; and sundry other editors
also waited and got their biographies ready, and
prayed that he might hang on till it was too late
for the evening papers and then die just in time
for the morning ones. The morning papers, &c., got
it as they generally do, for most monarchs (being
grateful for the morning paper grovel) die just in
time for the early editions, and Providence makes
up for it and tempers the wind to the shorn
evening paper by giving it the first show’ at the
hangings and floggings instead. The only editors
who were calm amid all the confusion were those
who ran two journals—morning and evening. If
a potentate died at night and a chimney sweep
were hanged in the morning they were ready for a
“ scoop ” in either contingency.

Mrs. Frances Baylor, an American
novelist, complains that there is no ‘‘home life ”in
her country, nowadays, because the wives arealways
shopping, dining-out, or attending publicmeetings.
From wliich it is evident that the fair dame
knows nothing of America outside the big cities.
There was plenty of home life in “ The County
Fair,” for instance.
The editor of Harper’s Weekly has com-
menced to arrest for false pretences those literary
confidence-operators who crib old poems and
stories and endeavour to sell them to him as new
matter. These people are not much more dis-
honest than certain fat proprietors we could name,
who, having refused to buy Australian literary
matter when tendered in original MS., calmly
proceed to steal it, without acknowledgment,
when they find it printed in the English papers.
Pomborneit (Vic.) rabbit-preserving fac-
tory employs 70 hands during the busy season,
and distributes in wages and the purchase of
rabbits about .£400 weekly. With present
facilities and a full staff, 20,000 pairs of rabbits or
8000 sheep can be tinned weekly. The first year
that the factory was opened something like
600,000 rabbits were handled, but not one-sixth of
that number can now be obtained, even over an
increased area. The trappers get fivepence per
pair for all rabbits delivered.
They elected a new Mayor the other day
in Dunedin (M.L.) and when he was safely in his
committee forwarded their little bill. The chair-
man charged £50 for his own services, .£20 for a
secretary, <£6 for an under-secretary, £5 10s. for
clerical assistance, and £113s. for pens, ink, and
stationery. Also, there was a charge for something
like 3800 drinks, and down near the bottom of the
invoice came an unobtrusive memo, which blushed
unseen to this effect: “ Four hats (for bets) £4
4s.” The Mayor refused to pay, and the com-
mittee say that he is a hog and are suing him for
the money.
A gentleman who signs himself “Boiling
Billy," and who states that for the past 25 years
he has only escaped finding work by the special
interposition of Providence, sends us sundry sug-
gestions for a new law code, as follows : —
(1) In time of strike it shall be unlawful for any person
to carry firearms, unless he be a paid-up member of some
labour organisation. Caution: Do not use them un-
necessarily. When you do use them, fire low.
(2) Any person being possessed of property exceeding
£20,000 in value, shall pay £100 per week into the strike
fund while strike continues: exceeding £20,000, such sum
as Mr. W. G. Spence shall direct.
(3) Any person or persons who shall feloniously or
otherwise assault, knock-about, stone, kill, or crush, or
in any way mangle or disembowel any squatter or squat-
ters without just cause, shall be handed over to Mr. \V.
G. Spence to be dealt with as he shall think proper.
(4) Anyone who shall be found guilty of killing another
shall be hanged by the neck till he is dead, dead,
deceased, excepting he shall prove deceased to have been
a scab or blackleg. Men are requested not to burn such
scab’s house or destroy his wife and children without a
special permit.
(6) Government shall supply rations free of charge to
all holders of union tickets.
(6) Any member of the police-force found guilty of
being involved in anv struggle between union men and
scabs shall—failing to prove that he was engaged in pro
tecting union men from bodily violence—be immediately
discharged from the force.
One or two clauses of an unreasonable and blood-
thirsty character have been struck out.
When, after the defeat of Balmaceda’s
people, the revelry in Valparaiso had partially
subsided, the bodies of the two dead Generals were
allowed to lie uncared-for for nearly a week :
Then they were picked up, placed on a rude, four-
wheeled carry-all, and dragged through the streets, being
spat upon and stoned by the infuriated mob. It was a
terrible sight to see the two bodies, stiff in death, the
rigid arm of one of them stretched out almost upright,
being treated in such a hideously uncivilised manner.
Balmaceda knew what he was about when he blew out Ins
brains rather than fall into the hands of these barbarous
wretches. And he was no better than they, for while he
bad powfer he was as ruthless to his enemies as they would
have been to him. He shot and tortured while he was
able, like a true Chileno, and his lieutenants followed his
example, after the custom of the country. A witness
says: “ It is sickening even to pen it; but I saw the mob
in Valparaiso fight for a bone, a hand, a small piece of
flesh, anything, out of the carts containing the bodies of
the Generals. And six officers of the Junta looked on and
smiled.”
A young gentleman from the country
called the other day at the Federal Bank (Melb.)
and asked, in a light and airy, but courteous tone
if he could see the manager. The manager was
visible and shouted, “ Come in !” to the stranger’s
knock. “How d’ye do, Mr. ?” said the
gentlemanly young person with the sun-burnt
face and the'three-months’ beard. “How d’ye
do, sir?" said the manager, rising politely and
pointing’ to a chair, “ take a seat, sir ; and what
can I do for you?’ he added affably, with a
latter-day bank manager’s keen scent for new
customers. “ Well, you don’t seem to know me,
Mr. ?'’ exclaimed the young man with pathos
in his voice. “No,” said the manager, “I con-
fess, sir, that you have the advantage of me. Mai
I enquire—-■?” “Oh, certainly—my name is
Fourdrinier, late teller in this bank, decamped
with fourteen hundred two or three months ago ;
I’ve only called to give myself up " bo it is .
Fourdrinicr ! Very glad to see yon, my boy ;
just touch that bell at your elbow, will you?
“With pleasure.” (Enter Buttons.) Bthd
bert, fetch a policeman,” remarks Mr. , but
the visitor objects—“ Oh, hang it, no ! a hansom :
von can drive with me to the detective office.
I’ve been rubbing shoulders with the d. s down
the Bay and all over tin' blooming place for tlv
last month, and as the money’s all gone they’ll Ik
>dad enough to ram me into ohokey.” Then thej
went out together, Fourdrinicr nodding plea-
santly to his former comrades on the way.

A crank goes about Collins-st. (Mel.)selling
millennium pamphlets, while his hat is ornamented
with a mitred proclamation, “Work and Pray.”
Now he hoists “ The Frogs Have Come ”—satis-
factory evidence that the Book of Revelation is
moving on, and it keeps one of the unemployed,
anyhow.

A muddy; hatlesg than rushed wildly into
a house at Lismore (N.3.W.), the other day, ahd
breathlessly informed the woman inside that her
husband had just been killed about half-a-mile
down the road. Then the poor, loving woman
uttered an eerie scream and ran down the road,
and while she was away looking for the remains,
the visitant cleaned out the premises and departed.
The S.M. Herald, under’ the heading of
“ The Australian Association for the Advance-
ment of Science ” :
Baron Von Mueller, in seconding the motion, said that
the map of Central Australia was being rapidly filled up,
until little would be left to be explored. With the excep-
tion of New Guinea, it was one of the most interesting
and fascinating countries of the world, not merely on
account of its extent of fertile land, and the enormous
height of its mountains, but because of the promises held
out by its hundreds of miles of rocks of an auriferous
character.
This is indeed news !
A veteran of 70 odd years Was the other
day admitted into Sydney Hospital, and put to
bed, with the clear conviction on the part of the
attendants that he would never leave the couch
fexcept feet-first. The previous bath, the titivat-
ing, and the clean sheets, combined to form a
restful haven for the old fellow', who presently
sought to cap his enjoyment by filling his old
clay and puffing away contentedly. “ You
carn’t smoke here! ” broke upon his dream,
like the croak of a crow. “You go to Heligo-
land,” was his prompt retort to the wardsman
who was the offender. “I tell yer, yer carn’t
smoke here.” “But I can, ’cos I am.” The
wardsman fetched the matron, who gently corro-
borated the former’s dictum—“ Smoking is not
allowed in the wards.” “ Ho, it hain't; hain’t it?”
yapped the old fellow, springing out of bed like a
12-year-old, “ then, by ’eavens, I’m going to a
houtside ’orspital, where I can smoke myself black
in the face, and empty me pooch, and cadge
more when I wanter.’* And he dressed himself
and left.
Before the G.N. Railway (N.S.W.) was
extended to the Queensland border Cobb and Co.
had in their employ for many years a coach-
driver who differed so from all other drivers as to
earn for himself the nickname of “Silent Bob.”
A loquacious drummer, on his first trip North,
took his seat on the box of Bob’s coach after the
arrival of the train late at night. When they
were fairly started on their journey over the
Moonbies he began to spin some of his best yarns.
At the end of the first he got half a grunt, half a
growl, instead of the expected laugh. He tried
Bob with another, with even less success. When
the hprses were just crawling along the most dan-
gerous part of the road the jolly bagman produced
the pick of the basket in the line of yarns and fired
it at the driver. Not a sound from the motionless
jehu. • Thinking he had grasped the situation,
the now-alarmed commercial asked, in startled
tones, “Are you asleep, driver—are you asleep?”
Like the blast of a fog-horn the answer came—
“ What the blanky blank would I be doing asleep,
you blanky fool !”

The Theatre Royal Hotel, .Sydney, has
lately changed hands, and is now under entirely
new management. A splendid stock of wines,
spirits, liqueurs and cigars has been laid in, and
an altogether new feature is provided in the shape
of an American bar, where all the strange, curious
and refreshing drinks known in the United States
can be obtained. Their name is legion, but among
them may be mentioned champagne cocktails,
gin fizz, milk punch, mint julep—probably the
noblest liquor known to man—imperial punch.
Manhattan cocktails, and many others. All
mixed by American bar-keeps. James A. Walsh,
proprietor, late of the Occidental Hotel, San
Francisco.*

The late John Probert, of Auckland (M.L.),
was a fair specimen of a mean advertising
religionist. He was a very wealthy man and a
Wesleyan ; and when he was seized with his last
illness the Wesleyan parsons gathered in great
force round his bed, and a Wesleyan lawyer drew
his will, and when he was dead it was found that
the Wesleyan Church had “scooped the pool.”
Then there was the usual outpouring in the press
about the munificent Christian who had departed,
and the dead benefactor was boomed in big black
print because he had hung on to his money like
grim death to the very last, and then had given it
to the Lord, and left an old, broken-down brother
and his wife dead-broke. This vetlerable pair now
potter along on 7s. (id. a week and feel bad
towards the deceased saint, and when they can
raise a stamp they write to the boss deacon asking
for some of the defunct millionaire’s money, and
the Church puts their letter in the waste-paper
basket and goes on its way in peace.

covery an abominable conspiracy. We say it was
abombinable.
There is a citizen of Sydney so stout that
he has to feed off his lap. He has passed the
stage at which lie could arrange matters by cutting
out a semi-circular piece of the table. Also, there
is another citizen so stout that he hasn’t had any
lap for the past ten years.
Lever Brothers, the manufacturers of
famous Sunlight soap; adopt a hovel, method d
fixing the rentals of their workmen’s cottages ■
The rent is reduced each successive year by 25 pe r
cent. Thus a steady workman can, after a few
years service* live rent-free* The employers
gain by the absence of friction among their hands j
and the hands have a perpetual incentive to good
work; and freeddiil from the Parking dread
of renCday. AS a step,towards industrial justice
Messrs. Lever’s plan is to be heartily c'dirimended;
A man strolled down into a Grafton
(N.S.W.) bar during the hot Christmastide,
and deposited a sixpenny bit for a “long
beer,” which was duly served to him. As
it gurgled down, he was observed to shiver,
but immediately resumed the operation of drink-
ing in marked haste, put down the glass without
a word, and with pursed lips walked hurriedly
outside. When round a corner he spat out a score
or so of sixpences into his hat. The barman had,
in the rush of trade, served out the wanderer’s
liquor in the glass in which he Usually kept his
small change*

It is worthy to be noted,
If you want to be promoted
To hold a little office in the Civil Service corpi,
It is wholly ineffectual
To say you’re intellectual,
The merest hint of such a thing, they’ll show you to the
door.
What they want are men of muscle.
Fully equal to a tussle
With a wretched individual whose bringing up was bad •
What they want are men of gristle
With a penchant for the whistle
Of the blood-bespattered cat upon some naked, moaning
lad.
So, if you cancross-cut sweetly,
Change your strokes both quick and neatly
From right to left and vice versa up along the ribs,
You will gain a post of honour
’Neath the banner of O’Connor,
Who blindly weighs out justice in the Ministry of Dibbs.
He conducts examination
In the art of flagellation,
With questions anatomic re the structure of the cat,
And wherefore, why and which is
The one that mostly itches?
(You’d think the man who’s flayed would be a connoisseur
in that).
If you pass “Pons Asinomm,'’
In the latest way to score ’em
And solve a triangle and show the neatest style of rack,
He’ll give you a diploma,
For you know as blind as Homer
Is that dame who wields the sword and scales at R.
O'Connor’s back.
Yet, still in our opinion
’Tisn’t Justice that a minion
Whose one recommendation is ability to wale,
With the cat no doubt secreted
On his person, should be seated
In office, though it only be a far-off country gaol.
